# Nathan Summers — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# EXECUTIVE BRIEF (BLUF)

## UATU DOSSIER — NATHAN CHRISTOPHER CHARLES SUMMERS

*Classification: Total-Spectrum Longitudinal // Subject Designation: CABLE // Status: Recurrently Active Across Temporal Vectors*

---

## I. BOTTOM LINE UP FRONT

**The subject is a weapon that refused its specification, and has spent every subsequent hour of a subjectively centenarian life trying to ensure no other child is ever specified the same way.**

That is the sentence. Everything else in this file — the cybernetics, the floating nation-state, the paramilitary training of minors, the sixteen-year wilderness paternity of Hope Summers, the death-and-return cycle, the murderous twin, the chronic terminal infection metabolized as a daily discipline — is corollary, consequence, or compensation.

Nathan Summers was conceived in a laboratory as countermeasure ordnance, infected in infancy by the very enemy he was made to oppose, surrendered by his father at perhaps six months of age to a religious order two thousand years downstream, raised as a messiah, replaced by his own clone, orphaned in adolescence, conscripted into a millennial war, returned to his own past as a grizzled cybernetic combatant older than the parents who gave him up, and proceeded — with full self-awareness — to spend his adult life staging the conditions of his own childhood in order to do them *correctly* this time. The diagnostic core is not the trauma. The diagnostic core is the **reparative iteration**: he keeps building the room he was born into, and rearranging the furniture until a child can survive it.

He has succeeded exactly once. Her name is Hope. He died finishing the job. He is, by his own ledger — the only one he respects — solvent.

---

## II. THE THROUGH-LINE

Across sixty-plus years of published existence and a subjective lifespan exceeding a century when one totals the Askani childhood, the future war years, the present-day operational decades, the Soldier X wandering, the sixteen-year flight with Hope across cauterized timelines, and the subsequent resurrections, there is **one continuous narrative spine** under all the costume changes and codenames:

> *A child taken from his parents is trying to give back to the world a child who was not.*

This is the load-bearing thesis. Every era of the subject's existence is a variation on it.

- **Askani'son era:** the child *is* taken. He learns the shape of the wound from the inside.
- **New Mutants / X-Force command:** he takes other people's children and trains them as combatants — repetition compulsion, with the partial-mitigation that he stays present where his own caretakers vanished. Imperfect reparation; he is replicating Apocalypse's logic in service of opposing Apocalypse, and he knows it, and the knowing does not stop him, only slows him.
- **Providence:** an attempt at the larger-scale solution — build the world that does not produce weaponized children. Surrendered when it began to *become* the thing it opposed. He has learned to dismantle his own projects before they consume their purpose; this is the single most underappreciated maturation in his file.
- **Soldier X / depowered wandering:** the depressive interlude. The reparative project has failed at scale; the body is failing; he retreats into ascetic motion, which is the only form of stillness available to him.
- **Hope Summers / sixteen-year flight:** the thesis executed perfectly. One child, total presence, no surrogates, no abandonment, no doctrine — *he stayed*. He died of it. He considers this a fair price; the file substantiates that he considers it the only price that was ever going to clear the ledger.
- **Kid Cable / Krakoa-era:** the younger self murders the older self and inherits the work. Read this carefully: even his own replacement is on-thesis. A child takes the place of the man who failed to protect himself in childhood, and is given the chance to do the run over from an earlier developmental position. The subject has, in effect, granted himself the parenting he was denied — by becoming, again, the child.

The through-line is therefore not heroism, not war, not prophecy. It is **the recursive attempt to repair the moment of surrender in X-Factor #68** — the moment Scott Summers handed an infant to a stranger from the future because the alternative was watching him die. Every Cable narrative is a re-staging of that handoff with different vectors of who hands whom to whom, and each iteration is scored on whether the child arrives intact on the other side.

---

## III. THE CORE WOUND AND ITS MAINTENANCE

### The Wound

The core wound is **not** the techno-organic infection. The infection is the *vehicle* of the wound, not the wound itself. The core wound is the **double abandonment with no available villain to blame**:

1. **Loss of biological father (infancy):** Scott surrendered him out of love, to save his life. There is no one to be angry at. The rage has no target and therefore lives in the body.
2. **Loss of surrogate parents (Slym and Redd / Scott and Jean, age ~12):** ripped from him by forces beyond anyone's control, again with love, again with no villain.

The structural cruelty of the subject's origin is that **every abandonment was performed by people who loved him, for his benefit, with no alternative.** This forecloses the ordinary developmental work of mourning, because mourning requires an identifiable loss and, ideally, an identifiable failure to hold against. He has neither. He has only the brute fact: *they were not there.* And the corollary insight, which the file shows him reaching repeatedly and never fully digesting: *if love does not prevent abandonment, then love is not protection, and protection must be manufactured by other means.*

This is the engine of the entire personality. Protection-manufacture is the lifework. Cybernetic augmentation, paramilitary training, precognitive strategic planning, telekinetic disease-suppression, the founding of a sovereign nation, the curation of timelines — these are all **technologies of unabandonment**, deployed because the original technology (parental love) was insufficient.

### The Maintenance

The wound is maintained by four interlocking mechanisms, each of which the subject would describe — if asked, which he would not permit — as a necessary operational adaptation:

**1. The body as continuous reminder.** The T-O virus requires hourly, conscious telekinetic suppression. He cannot forget that he is dying, was given away, was infected, was constructed. The disease functions as an embodied mnemonic of origin. He cannot dissociate from the wound because the wound is metabolizing him in real time. *This is why he does not drink, does not numb, does not rest:* unconsciousness is non-suppression is death. His vigilance is not a personality trait; it is a life-support protocol that has colonized the personality.

**2. The mirror-twin.** Stryfe exists. The clone raised as Apocalypse's heir is the externalized answer to the question *what would I have become if the other handoff had happened?* Cable cannot resolve his identity without resolving Stryfe, and Stryfe cannot be resolved because he is, in the strictest psychoanalytic sense, a part of the self projected outward and given autonomous narrative agency. The feud is the wound externalized and made tactical.

**3. The unrepairable parental relationship.** Reunited with Scott as an adult who is biologically older than his own father, the developmental work of being-parented is structurally foreclosed. Scott cannot parent a man with grey hair and a cybernetic arm who has commanded armies; Nathan cannot be parented by a man younger than himself. The role-inversion is permanent. They love each other tactically. This is the closest configuration available, and both of them know it is not enough, and neither will say so. The file is unambiguous on this point: every Summers-Summers interaction in the canon carries the freight of an unfinishable conversation.

**4. The messianic structure he cannot fully refuse.** The Askani raised him as the Chosen One. He resists the framing publicly throughout adulthood. But Providence happened. The Hope project happened. Cable acts, structurally, as a messiah while denying he is one. The reaction formation is load-bearing: to fully accept the role would be to ratify the cult that took him from his parents; to fully reject it would be to nullify the meaning of everything that was done to him. He lives in the gap, which is the gap the wound occupies.

---

## IV. WHO HE IS AT THE DEEPEST LEVEL

Strip every layer — the gear, the war record, the prophecy, the cybernetics, the codenames, the strategic apparatus — and the man underneath is a **grieving, ethically rigorous, lonely person with chronic pain and no permission to stop.**

He is not cold. He presents cold because affective expression was, in his developmental environment, militarily unsafe and physically expensive (every erg spent on emotion is an erg not spent suppressing the virus). The flatness is a thermal regulation system, not the absence of heat.

He is not arrogant. He presents commanding because he learned early that hesitation in leadership gets the people behind you killed, and he has been responsible for people-behind-him since before puberty. The certainty is a duty discharged, not a satisfaction enjoyed.

He is not a warrior by disposition. He is a warrior by induction. The file shows, in every quiet moment the canon permits him, a man whose native register is **teacherly** — patient, demonstrative, oriented toward the student's autonomous mastery. The reason he kept building paramilitary units around teenagers is that teaching is the only intimacy he was ever offered and the only intimacy he knows how to offer back. War is the medium because war is the inheritance; teaching is the message.

He is not without faith. He is without faith *in institutions that promise to protect children*, having been the object of several such promises. He retains faith in individual reparative action, in the long iterative arc of history, in the possibility of editing the future, and — quietly, never spoken — in his father, whom he has forgiven for the original handoff, because as a father himself now he understands the calculus and would have made the same call.

He is, at the deepest level, **a man trying to discharge an inheritance.** The inheritance is: a body built as a weapon, a destiny written by a cult, a war started before his birth, two parents he could not keep, a twin engineered to be his negation, and a future he is uniquely positioned to prevent. He treats all of this as **a job left for him on a desk.** He intends to finish it before he goes. He has been "going" — dying, withdrawing, being murdered by his own younger self, being resurrected — for decades, and the desk is not yet clear. He continues.

---

## V. OPERATIONAL SUMMARY

**Threat profile:** High-capability, ethically governed, low-volatility, long-planning. Will not initiate violence outside mission scope. Will exceed any threshold of personal cost to complete a mission scoped to the protection of non-combatants, particularly minors. Lethal force employed instrumentally and proportionally; no evidence of gratification, considerable evidence of subsequent moral accounting.

**Predictability:** High on principle, low on tactics. *What* he will do in a given ethical scenario can be forecast with confidence. *How* he will do it is constrained only by his strategic creativity, which is substantial and operates on timelines other actors cannot model.

**Manageability:** Negligible. He cannot be handled, recruited under false pretenses, manipulated by appeals to authority, or coerced via threats to his person. The single working lever is a credible threat to a child in his orbit, which produces compliance up to the moment he can engineer the child's safety, after which the lever ceases to exist and the wielder of it is in immediate and severe danger.

**Recruitability:** He recruits. He is not recruited. He will accept temporary tactical alignment with parties whose objectives intersect his own and will withdraw without notice when intersection ends. Allies are advised to assume he is operating on three timelines they cannot see.

**Decompensation profile:** Withdrawal, ascetic motion, depressive flattening. Will not act out, will not lash out, will not collapse in place. Disappears. Reappears when the situation requires him. The Soldier X period is the template.

**Terminal-state behavior:** Martyrdom-coded. Self-expenditure to clear an obligation. The Hope handoff is the template. Resurrection has not modified this; if anything, the repeated returns have hardened his willingness to spend the body, because the body has demonstrated it can be regrown and the obligations cannot.

**Critical vulnerabilities:** 
- A child credibly endangered in his presence overrides all strategic discipline.
- Confrontation with the X-Force critique — that his methods reproduce his enemies' — lands, because it is true, and he knows.
- The Stryfe complex remains unresolved and likely unresolvable; sustained engagement degrades judgment.
- Offers of rest read as pre-abandonment cues and produce immediate trust collapse.

**Compatibility profile:** Functional in asymmetric relationships (mentor, commander, father, prophet). Degraded in symmetric ones (peer, partner, sibling). The Domino exception proves the rule: she has accepted that she will do the emotional labor and has built a relationship around that contract. The Scott relationship is permanently inverted and cannot mature. The Hope relationship is his masterwork.

**Long-term trajectory:** Indefinite. The subject does not retire. The subject does not stop. The subject will continue to appear at inflection points across temporal vectors, do the necessary work, absorb the cost personally, and either die finishing or vanish to recover for the next inflection. He will continue to attempt the reparative iteration. He will, on the metric he respects — *are the children I was responsible for still alive* — continue to win more than he loses.

---

## VI. THE ONE-PARAGRAPH FILE

Nathan Christopher Charles Summers is a man designed as a weapon, infected as an infant, exiled as a baby, raised as a messiah, replaced by a clone, orphaned in adolescence, soldiered into manhood across a millennium of war, returned to his own past as a stranger older than his own father, given chronic terminal illness as a permanent companion, denied the relief of unconsciousness because unconsciousness is death, denied the relief of intimacy because intimacy was the original wound, and granted exactly one true vocation — the protection of children from the conditions that made him — which he has pursued with such ferocity, longevity, and self-cost that the only honest assessment is that he has been, despite everything, **right**. The repair worked. Hope survived. The work continues. The subject continues. The desk is not clear. He has not asked to be relieved.

*— UATU, observing.*


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# CHRONOLOGY I — ORIGIN & CHILDHOOD

*Subject: Nathan Christopher Charles Summers. Period covered: pre-conception (Mr. Sinister's breeding program, late 1980s publication / "decades prior" in-universe) through the end of the Askani'son cycle and his return to the late 20th century as an adult combatant. Sources: Uncanny X-Men #201, #239–243; X-Factor #13–68 ("Inferno" arc, particularly #67–68); New Mutants #87; Adventures of Cyclops & Phoenix #1–4; Askani'son #1–4; The Further Adventures of Cyclops and Phoenix #1–4; Cable #-1 ("Flashback"); Cable: Blood and Metal #1–2; assorted retrospective inserts across the Cable solo (1993–2002).*

---

## 1. PRE-NATAL ENGINEERING — THE FOUNDATIONAL FACT

The subject did not begin as a child; he began as a project. Nathaniel Essex — operating under the pseudonym Mr. Sinister and with the long resources of a Victorian-trained geneticist who has had a century to refine method — designed Madelyne Pryor as a vehicle for a specific gestation. Pryor was a clone of Jean Grey engineered, conditioned, and inserted into Scott Summers's life with the express purpose of producing a child whose genome would unify the Summers and Grey lines (*Uncanny* #239–241; *X-Factor* #38 reveals). The breeding program is articulated, in canon, as a centuries-long Apocalypse-countermeasure: Sinister wanted a weapon proportionate to a god.

**Operational consequence for the subject:** Nathan's existence is not contingent on the love of his parents. It is contingent on a third party's strategic forecast. Every later attempt at family attachment is structured against this baseline — the knowledge, eventually conscious, that he was *commissioned* before he was conceived. The Askani prophecy that frames his childhood will simply repeat the Sinister design at a different theological register. Two cults of expectation, separated by two millennia, agreeing on him. He never escapes the sensation of being scheduled.

---

## 2. BIRTH AND EARLIEST INFANCY (X-Factor #42, "Inferno" framework)

Born during what is, in publication-historical terms, *Inferno* — a literal demonic invasion of New York. The delivery itself occurs in conditions of supernatural siege. Madelyne, by this point partially possessed by the Goblin Queen pattern that Sinister and the demon-lords N'astirh and S'ym have activated in her, is targeted to use the infant as the focus of a portal-opening blood ritual. The first hours of the subject's life are spent strapped to an altar.

Behavioral inference (from a surveillance posture): *the body keeps the score in the pre-verbal nervous system.* Cable's adult somatic profile — chronic baseline hyperarousal, startle suppressed but elevated heart-rate persistent, sleep architecture fragmented and militarized — is consistent with a child whose first sensory environment was acute parental distress and ambient violence. There is no benign infancy in the file. There is no "before."

He is rescued by Scott, Jean, and the combined X-Factor/X-Men forces. Madelyne dies (Goblin Queen suicide / Jean reabsorption, *X-Factor* #38; *Uncanny X-Men* #243). The infant is now in his father's custody, in a household — X-Factor's headquarters — that is itself a converted Celestial ship, run by a paramilitary research team. *The first stable address is a military base.*

---

## 3. THE INFECTION — X-Factor #67–68

Apocalypse, recognizing the threat the child is calibrated to represent, kidnaps him and infects him with the techno-organic virus (the same nanotechnological pathogen carried by Apocalypse's Riders and, in earlier appearances, by Warlock's species). The virus's behavior is well-established in the medical section of this file; what matters here is the **clinical-developmental** picture.

The child is now:
- Visibly disfigured along the left side (early colonization of the orbit, shoulder, arm).
- In continuous pain, expressed in infant terms (uncodified, somatic, undifferentiable from hunger or cold).
- Subject to extended medical handling by adults who cannot save him.

Scott's decision — to give the infant to Sister Askani, who arrives from 2,000 years in the future claiming the child can be cured only there — is, in attachment-theory terms, the **first abandonment**. The intent is paternal love. The delivered experience is: *the largest, most familiar figure hands me to a stranger and does not come.* The infant nervous system does not distinguish motive from outcome.

**Annotation for the file:** Every subsequent moment of Cable's life in which he chooses to do something *for* his loved ones that they will *experience as betrayal* — sending Hope home alone, fleeing forward in time without explaining, recruiting children to X-Force "for their own development" — recapitulates this transaction from the other side of the handoff. He learned the grammar of protective abandonment in his cradle and has been speaking it ever since.

---

## 4. ARRIVAL IN THE ASKANI FUTURE (Askani'son #1; Adventures of Cyclops & Phoenix #1)

The infant arrives in the 38th century, on an Earth ruled (in functional terms) by the cult of Apocalypse, with pockets of Askani resistance. The Clan Askani — a matriarchal psionic order founded by the future adult Rachel Summers in her Mother Askani persona — receives the child and immediately frames him in eschatological terms: he is "Dayspring," he is the "Askani'son," he is the prophesied vessel by which Apocalypse will be undone.

A two-year-old does not parse "messiah." He parses:
- *I am held by women who chant when they touch me.*
- *They are afraid of me and they are afraid of losing me.*
- *Some of the older ones cry when they see my eye.*

The Clan stabilizes the virus enough that he will live, but cannot cure it. The disease is now a permanent companion — and, critically, the **first object he learns to control with his mind.** Early psionic training in the Askani method is structured precisely as a meditative containment of the T-O spread. Before he can read, he is doing what amounts to advanced biofeedback against a terminal infection. *His first conscious use of his mutation is medical self-defense.* This is the origin of his lifelong association of psionic power with **labor, vigilance, and the prevention of personal annihilation** — not with wonder, or with play, or with connection to others. Other telepaths grow into their gift; he grew into a tourniquet.

---

## 5. THE STRYFE SUBSTITUTION (Askani'son #2; The Adventures of Cyclops and Phoenix #2–3)

At some point in early childhood — canonically before age four — Apocalypse's forces raid the Askani sanctuary. A clone of Nathan, prepared by Apocalypse's geneticists as a contingency, is in play. In the confusion, the two infants are separated. The clone (later: Stryfe) is taken by Apocalypse's people and raised as the heir; Nathan remains with the Askani.

**For Nathan, the lived experience is subtler than the plot summary.** He does not, as a small child, know that there is a duplicate of himself being raised in a fortress somewhere being told he is the chosen one. What he knows is:
- A whispered uncertainty among his caretakers about *which child they have.*
- Periodic genetic/psionic tests run on him by Askani adepts, ostensibly devotional, functionally diagnostic.
- A baseline of being *looked at to be verified* rather than *looked at to be seen.*

The subject's adult intolerance for being "managed" by allies — flagged in the psychological section as an immediate trust-collapse trigger — traces here. The earliest social environment trained him to expect that the adults around him were running calculations on him while smiling. He learned to run his own in return.

---

## 6. THE TWELVE YEARS WITH SLYM AND REDD (Adventures of Cyclops and Phoenix #1–4; The Further Adventures of Cyclops and Phoenix #1–4)

The most extensively documented and developmentally pivotal period.

Scott Summers and Jean Grey, in the present, have their consciousnesses sent forward by Rachel/Mother Askani into temporary host bodies. They take the names **Slym Dayspring** and **Redd Dayspring** and assume guardianship of Nathan for approximately twelve subjective years (the texts are slightly inconsistent — somewhere between ten and twelve). They do not, initially, tell him who they are. He grows up calling them by their assumed names, in a small Askani-aligned domestic compound in the Sinsear wastes.

**This is the only stretch of stable two-parent family life the subject ever has.** Granular markers from the comics:

- Slym teaches him marksmanship, hand-to-hand, and tactical patience. The use of small targets at progressively longer ranges. The discipline of waiting.
- Redd teaches him psionic containment, meditation, and — critically — the ethical syllabus of the Askani: *what one does with power that exceeds others'.* She is the source of the moral seriousness that will later distinguish him from every other heavily-armed time-traveler in his cohort.
- Domestic detail: he learns to cook over open fires; he is taught to repair his own gear; he is forbidden from leaving the perimeter alone; he has a pet (canon: a sand-cat analog in Askani'son).
- He is taught to read from salvaged 20th-century texts the Askani have preserved. *He grows up reading our books in our future.*
- He is told, at some point in the late period, who his parents truly are. The revelation is staged; it does not undo the prior years of intimacy under assumed names.

Then, with no warning calibrated to his understanding, Slym and Redd are pulled back to the present. Their host bodies collapse. **He is, at roughly age twelve, alone.** This is the **second abandonment** — and unlike the first, it is one he remembers, can name, and will carry consciously the rest of his life.

Behavioral aftermath, derivable from later character behavior:
- Lifelong difficulty with terminations of relationship. He does not say goodbye to people; he disappears, or he stages the disappearance of others. He learned that goodbyes are something done *to* you while you sleep.
- Permanent association of *parental love* with *imposture*. The two people who loved him best did so under false names. Authenticity, in his interpersonal grammar, is suspect; performance under necessary cover is the norm. This is why he can mentor and parent so effectively under operational pretense (X-Force, Providence, Hope-on-the-run) and so poorly in unstructured peacetime.
- A specific reading habit, persistent in adulthood: 20th-century paper books, salvaged or replica. Domino references his "antique book collection" in the Nicieza run. He is reading the texts of his foster parents' homeworld. He never stopped.

---

## 7. WAR-CAMP ADOLESCENCE (Askani'son #3–4; Cable: Blood and Metal #1; Cable #-1)

From approximately twelve to early adulthood, Nathan is a soldier in the Clan Askani resistance. The texts are episodic but consistent on environment:

- **Constant mobility.** No address held longer than weeks. He learns to sleep in shifts, to pack inside ninety seconds, to never own anything he cannot carry.
- **Companions die.** Cable #-1 details the loss of childhood friends and lovers in skirmishes against Apocalypse's forces. He internalizes the lesson that attachment in wartime is an ambush of the self.
- **He commands young.** By his late teens he is leading patrols. The pattern of **placing other children in danger because the war requires it** — the X-Force critique that will haunt him — is established here as a survival norm, not a vice. He did not invent it. He inherited it. He will, in adulthood, replicate it under conditions where he had alternatives, and this is the difference between trauma and complicity.
- **First killing:** off-page, but referenced; characterized in his interior monologue (Cable solo, various) as an unremarkable transition. He notes the absence of a notable feeling. The absence is the noteworthy datum.

He meets the historical figure of Blaquesmith during this period — a wizened mentor-figure of indeterminate species who becomes a long-running off-stage advisor. Blaquesmith functions as a replacement parent of a third kind: not biological (Scott), not under-cover (Slym), but **explicitly transactional and instructive.** Blaquesmith teaches strategy and the very-long view. This third paternal template is the one that holds — the one Cable will himself perform with Hope, with the New Mutants, with the teen version of himself. He learns that the most reliable form of love is *curriculum*.

---

## 8. FIRST CONFRONTATION WITH STRYFE AND THE QUESTION OF SELF (Askani'son #4; flashbacks across X-Cutioner's Song)

At some point in late adolescence or early adulthood, Nathan encounters Stryfe — and the visual and genetic identity is total. Each is told by his own faction that *he* is the original and the other is the abomination. Both believe it. The subject will not learn the truth (that he is the original) with certainty until *X-Cutioner's Song* in the present-day timeline, decades subjective later. He spends his entire developmental period with an unresolved question at the cellular level: **am I the real one, or am I the copy who was lied to?**

This is the foundational structure of the splitting defense flagged elsewhere in the file. He does not split good-self from bad-self in the ordinary borderline manner; he splits **authentic-self from substituted-self**, and the substituted-self walks around in his face shooting at him. The Stryfe relation is not a metaphor for an internal conflict. It is the externalization, in flesh, of the literal question he carried since childhood about whether he was who he had been told he was.

---

## 9. EARLIEST BEHAVIORAL PATTERNS — THE GROUND FLOOR

Drawing the threads of Sections 1–8 into a baseline behavioral inventory observable by adulthood and traceable, item by item, to childhood:

- **Perimeter vigilance.** Eyes to exits on entry to any room. Established: war-camp adolescence; reinforced: every subsequent decade.
- **Minimal sleep, fragmented.** Established: T-O infant management, when waking pain disrupted infant sleep architecture; calcified: war-camp years.
- **Possessions held to what one body can carry.** Established: mobile resistance period. Persistent into Providence era, where even as sovereign of a floating nation he maintains a personal kit reducible to one bag.
- **Reading habit, paper, 20th-century texts.** Established: Slym-and-Redd household. Sentimental and unconscious.
- **Aversion to being called by titles.** "Cable" is acceptable because it is operational; "Chosen One," "Askani'son," "Messiah" are not. Established: Askani childhood, where the titles preceded any verification of who he was.
- **Use of psionics as containment rather than expression.** Established: infant T-O management.
- **Affection delivered as instruction.** Established: Blaquesmith template, ratified across all adult mentoring relationships.
- **Termination of relationships by disappearance rather than farewell.** Established: Slym and Redd's vanishing.
- **Identification of family with imposture-under-necessity.** Established: same.
- **Tactical patience on multi-year and multi-decade horizons.** Established: Askani religious cosmology, which trained him from boyhood to think in 2,000-year arcs; reinforced by Slym's marksmanship pedagogy of *wait, wait, wait, then once.*
- **Refusal of rest framed as ethical principle.** Established: T-O infancy — *rest equals death* — recoded in adolescence as *rest equals others die.*
- **Tolerance for pain so high it is no longer informative.** Established: continuous infant and childhood viral pain, which he had to learn to ignore in order to function. He never learned to listen to his body again.

---

## 10. CLOSING NOTE ON THE PERIOD

The subject leaves childhood — the Askani'son cycle closes — as a young man in command of small units, a survivor of two distinct parental losses, a carrier of a terminal disease that he has personally suppressed since before he could speak, a religious figure to a cult he half-believes and half-resents, and the mirror-twin of a man who wants to kill him for occupying the role that man believes is his. He has been raised by, in succession: his biological parents (months), a militarized super-team headquarters (months), an apocalyptic god-tyrant's medical staff (days), a future religious order's nursery (years), his own time-displaced biological parents under assumed identities (twelve years), the resistance war machine (years), and a mentor of uncertain species (open-ended).

No single adult was present for the entirety of his childhood. He himself was the only continuous witness to his own upbringing.

This is the ground floor. Every subsequent chronology section in this file — the return to the present in *New Mutants* #87, the founding of X-Force, Providence, the years with Hope, the deaths and resurrections — is built on it. The man cannot be read without it. The boy was never not a soldier, was never not a patient, was never not a prophecy, and was never, in the ordinary sense of the word, a child.


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# CHRONOLOGY II — FORMATION & EMERGENCE

*Subjective years approximately 8 through 30. Geographic theater: the post-Apocalyptic North American littoral, ca. 3700–3800 A.D., with subsequent displacement across multiple branch timelines. All ages are subjective; chronological age is meaningless for this subject and will be flagged only where the discrepancy is operationally relevant.*

---

## 6. THE CLAN ASKANI INTERREGNUM (subj. age ~8–12)

The child is conscious, by this point, of three facts that no eight-year-old has the developmental scaffolding to integrate:

1. He is, in the local cosmology, **the prophesied one** — addressed by title (*Dayspring*, *Askani'son*, *the Chosen*) rather than name, by adult women who kneel.
2. He is **infected**. The T-O conversion has stalled at the left side of his face and torso but is visibly mineralizing him. He has, by this age, learned to read the bloom: the way the metallic webbing crawls a millimeter forward when he is feverish, recedes a millimeter when he is calm. He learns to be calm. This is the origin of the affect-clamp that will define him as an adult.
3. He has been **handed over**. Not lost. Not abandoned by accident. *Given*. This distinction is critical and the subject worries it like a stone for the rest of his life.

The Clan Askani at this period is a militant theocratic cell — robed, ritualized, telepathically organized through the "Askani sisterhood" psionic lattice. They feed him, they school him, they prostrate themselves, and they will not touch him casually. He is raised at arm's length by people who treat him as sacred. **The deficit produced here is specific:** he has no experience of being held without ceremony. As an adult he will reproduce this exactly — the meaningful gesture is the hand on the shoulder before combat, never the embrace at rest.

His education in this period is bifurcated. By day: scripture (the Askani prophecies, much of which concerns him by name, an intolerable position for any child); psionic discipline drills (early-stage telekinetic shielding, taught explicitly as *medicine* — the virus-suppression mechanism is built into him as a religious practice before it is built as a clinical one); rudimentary armed combat. By night: the smuggled, hushed presence of two adults he is told to call **Slym and Redd** — a couple who arrive in the camp as refugees, who take a particular interest in him, who are unaccountably permitted unsupervised time with the messiah.

He does not know they are his parents. He knows that their presence is the only thing in his life that does not feel like a ceremony.

---

## 7. THE SLYM-AND-REDD INTERVAL (subj. ~10–12)

For a window of perhaps twenty-four to thirty subjective months, the subject has something approximating a childhood. Redd reads to him. Slym teaches him a fighting stance the Askani do not use — feet narrower, weight forward, optic-discipline (a tell: even in this future, even with no eye-beams, the father transmits the father's stance). They eat together. He laughs in recorded telepathic impressions retrievable from this period — a sound the adult subject will not produce in the present-day record more than a half-dozen times across thirty years of published surveillance.

He is, at this stage, an essentially normal boy with a tumor and a destiny.

Then they leave. They do not say goodbye in any sustained way; the temporal mechanism that recalled them was not negotiable, and the subject experiences it as **disappearance mid-sentence**. He is approximately twelve. The Askani sisters explain it to him in liturgical terms — *the Mother and Father were sent to prepare you, and now their work is done* — which lands as confirmation that even the warmth was instrumental. **Loss event #2** in the developmental ledger, and the one that does the most architectural damage, because it is the loss of the only relationship he had ever experienced as non-utilitarian, and it is reframed for him, by trusted authority, as having been utilitarian all along.

The compensatory belief he constructs in the months following — observable in later behavior — is this: *love that arrives is love that will be withdrawn on a schedule you do not control; therefore the only sustainable form of love is the kind you administer to others.* He becomes, from this point forward, a giver and not a receiver. This is not a personality trait. It is a survival heuristic encoded at age twelve.

---

## 8. THE ABDUCTION AND THE SUBSTITUTION (subj. ~12–13)

Within months of the Slym-Redd departure, **Apocalypse's forces locate the Clan Askani enclave**. The sisterhood is largely slaughtered. The subject is captured. In the same operation, the subject's clone — a genetically identical infant-into-child, grown to match — is extracted from Apocalypse's labs and **inserted in his place** as the false messiah.

The clone is named **Stryfe**. The clone is raised in the palaces. The clone is told he is the Chosen. The clone is given tutors, technology, prosthetics that work.

The original — the subject — is processed into Apocalypse's labor and conscript stratum. He spends the next several subjective years in **work camps and resistance cells**, alternately. He learns three things in this period that will never leave him:

- **The face in the mirror is not safe.** Stryfe is shown to him, eventually, as a calculated psychological assault — Apocalypse's officers stage the encounter. The subject sees himself in dress uniform, healthy, beloved, on the wrong side. He understands instantly the structural injustice — *that boy is me on a different schedule* — and also experiences the first wave of the lifelong question: *which of us is the real one*. The latter question never resolves. It is reopened in adulthood (X-Cutioner's Song) and will still be reopening at the time of his death(s).
- **Hierarchies of suffering are real and he is not at the top.** The camps contain children worse off than him. He develops, here, the moral spine that will later look like grim asceticism: *you do not complain in front of people whose situation is worse, and almost everyone's situation is worse*. The adult subject's near-total refusal to discuss his own pain has its root in a twelve-year-old's labor-camp discipline.
- **Adults will get you killed.** Resistance cells in this period are repeatedly betrayed, infiltrated, or simply incompetent. The subject survives several pogroms in which the operational leadership did not. He concludes — correctly, given his evidence — that **command is a function you assume by being the one still alive**, not a position bestowed. He will never again wait to be authorized.

---

## 9. THE FIRST POWER-EMERGENCE: CONTAINMENT AS MUTATION (subj. ~13–15)

The Omega-class genetic potential begins to express adolescently in the conventional X-gene timetable. But the expression in this subject is **atypical and tragic**: as the telepathic and telekinetic capacity comes online, it is immediately conscripted, autonomically, to suppress the T-O virus, which has been waiting for a power source.

The clinical picture, reconstructed: at approximately thirteen, the virus surges — a near-fatal episode, perhaps the first of several. The nascent telekinesis flares, walls the conversion off at the cellular level, and **never stands down again**. The subject's lived experience of his own mutancy is therefore not the experience most mutants describe (a new sense, a new capacity, a new self). It is the experience of **a draft notice**: the moment your powers manifest, they are commandeered for your own survival, and you will never know what it would feel like to use them freely.

This is the single most consequential biological fact in the subject's psychological formation. Every other mutant in the canonical record discovers in adolescence that they *can*. This subject discovers in adolescence that he *must*, and that the surplus — the small remainder above the suppression load — is all he will ever have to spend. He learns, immediately, to **budget**. The adult subject's strategic mind, the long-horizon planning, the refusal to expend resources except on calculated objectives — this is not temperament. This is what happens to a thirteen-year-old who learns that every joule of psionic energy spent on something fun is a joule not spent on staying alive.

The residual telekinesis he can deploy externally is, even so, considerable — sufficient to lift, to deflect, to shield small groups. He keeps it hidden in the camps. The Askani training in psionic discipline pays its dividend here: he can use the power without flaring it visibly, without telepathic bleed-off that would mark him to Apocalypse's psi-sniffers. **He learns covert operation as a literal psychic posture before he learns it as a tactic.**

---

## 10. THE CLAN CHOSEN AND THE FIRST COMMAND (subj. ~15–22)

By mid-adolescence the subject has surfaced into the organized resistance — initially as a foot-soldier, rapidly as a squad-leader, eventually as the field commander of the cell that will be called, in later records, the **Clan Chosen**. The promotion is not political. It is, as predicted in the prior section, the simple operation of being the one still alive after the prior commanders are not.

Observable behaviors from this period, drawn from later flashback and from the recorded operational habits that persist into adulthood:

- **He plans operations on whiteboards (or the future-equivalent) two to three contingencies deep, color-coded by casualty estimate.** The habit survives intact into the X-Force years. He will, as an adult, draw the same branching diagrams on a Westchester chalkboard that he drew at seventeen on a charred wall in Crestcoast.
- **He develops the speech pattern.** The flat, clipped, declarative cadence — short sentences, instructive verbs, no hedging — is a *command voice*, forged in environments where instructions had to land in one pass and could not be queried. The interesting observation is that he never modulates out of it. The adult subject speaks to lovers, to children, to allies, and to enemies in approximately the same register. This is not affect. This is the residue of seven years in which any other register would have gotten someone killed.
- **He acquires the body.** The asymmetric musculature, the favoring of the right side, the trained tolerance for the left-side T-O weight — all of this is laid down here. He is hauling roughly fifteen to twenty percent of his bodyweight in mineralized tissue and learning to fight with it. The fighting style that emerges is *grounded, economical, weight-forward, exploiting the T-O mass as a striking surface*. He never adopts a flashy style; he cannot afford one.
- **He acquires the gun.** The fetish for oversized firearms — read by later commentators as adolescent compensation, and they are not wrong, but they are incomplete — is laid down here. In a theater where the enemy is armored or augmented, the subject *cannot* afford to expend telekinesis on offense. The TK is doing the medical work. Kinetic-projectile weapons are how he fights when his powers are otherwise occupied. **The gun is the prosthesis for the powers he doesn't have access to.** This is operationally true; it is also psychologically true (he is a telekinetic who lives like an infantryman; the dissonance is structural).

He takes lovers in this period. The record is sparse but indicative: **Aliya**, an Askani-aligned resistance fighter, becomes his wife in late adolescence; they have a son, **Tyler**. The relationship is, by the standards available to him, healthy — Aliya is a peer, a soldier, someone who can call him on his own bullshit and does. Tyler is, briefly, a son raised in something resembling a family.

Stryfe kills Aliya. Stryfe takes Tyler.

**Loss events #3 and #4**, delivered by the mirror-self in the same operation. Tyler will resurface, years later, as the villain **Genesis**, attempting to inflict the T-O virus on others as an act of revenge against a father he believes abandoned him. The subject's grandson-by-architecture, his son-by-blood, weaponized against him in his own diagnostic image. The pattern — *children turned into weapons by the absence of their parents* — is the curse the subject will spend his adult life trying to break, and he can name the exact moment he first failed to break it.

---

## 11. THE ASKANI RECRUITMENT AND THE TEMPORAL JUMP (subj. ~25–30)

Sometime in his mid-to-late twenties, the surviving Askani sisterhood — reconstituted, depleted, hunted — re-establishes contact and discloses the truth: he is the original. Stryfe is the clone. The prophecy he was told as a child was about him; the boy who replaced him at twelve was a forgery. Apocalypse is, in the present timeline (their past, 2,000 years upstream), about to ascend to the position from which all of this future flows. The Askani have a single-use bodyslide protocol. One operative can be sent back. The mission is to kill Apocalypse in the cradle of his power, or, failing that, to organize the resistance that will eventually contain him.

The subject volunteers. He is the obvious choice — he has the genome, the training, the immunity to Apocalypse's psionic signature, and **he has nothing left in this timeline**. Aliya is dead. Tyler is missing or turned. The Clan Chosen is, by this point, a few dozen exhausted survivors. He goes.

**The defining choice of his young adulthood is therefore this:** he accepts a one-way passage into his own great-great-ancestral past, with a mission written in scripture by people who raised him as a god, against an enemy whose mirror image of him is also somewhere loose in time, and he goes alone. The choice is not heroic in any uncomplicated sense. It is the choice of a man who has run out of attachments in his present and is being offered a mission in another. **He does not stay because there is nothing to stay for.** This is important: the founding act of his adult career is not a calling. It is an evacuation dressed as a calling, and the adult subject will spend decades retroactively building the calling into something he can believe in.

---

## 12. ARRIVAL AND THE FIRST DEFINING CHOICES IN THE PRESENT (early "Cable" period)

He arrives in the present heavily kitted, subjectively middle-aged, in a body that the present's medicine cannot diagnose, carrying a weapon nobody recognizes, speaking a clipped militarized English with archaic loanwords from a language two millennia downstream. The first defining choices of the present-tense era — each of which casts a shadow across all subsequent decades:

**Choice 1: He does not approach his parents.** Scott Summers is alive, here, now, a peer-aged man. The subject could walk up to him. He does not. The reasons, layered: protection of the timeline; protection of Scott from the knowledge of what becomes of the infant; protection of himself from a reunion he cannot afford to want. The choice is correct, possibly, and it is also avoidant, and the avoidance is the dominant motive. **He cannot bear to be the older son of his own father.** It will be years before he allows the relationship to be acknowledged, and even then he manages it at distance.

**Choice 2: He takes command of the New Mutants.** Offered, in essence, a teaching position at Xavier's by circumstance, he converts it within months into a paramilitary command. The students he inherits — Cannonball, Boom-Boom, Sunspot, Warpath, Rictor — are reorganized as **X-Force**. They are children. He knows they are children. He trains them anyway. This is the **identification-with-the-aggressor** of the prior workup made flesh: the man who was a child soldier reproduces the conditions that produced him, with the modification — and he holds onto this modification as moral cover — *that he stays*. He is the commander who does not get extracted. He will be there in the morning. He believes this is enough. It is partially enough, and the partial is the wound.

**Choice 3: He keeps the secret.** He does not disclose, at this stage, to anyone, that he is Nathan Summers. He does not disclose the Askani prophecy framework. He does not disclose Stryfe. He operates with a unit whose members do not know their commander's name. The compartmentalization is operationally defensible and psychologically catastrophic — it locks in, at the entry point of his entire present-day life, the pattern that **the people he protects must never know the full price**. Every subsequent relationship will be constrained by what he laid down in this first choice.

**Choice 4: He fights Stryfe.** Within his first operational years in the present, the mirror-self arrives, having followed him upstream. The X-Cutioner's Song arc weaponizes the ambiguity that has lived in the subject since age twelve — *which of us is the real one* — by having Stryfe assert convincingly that **Stryfe** is Nathan Summers and the subject is the clone. The subject *believes him*, for a window of weeks-to-months, and the belief is observable in his behavior — a decompensation, a willingness to be killed, a kind of relief at being told he was not, after all, the prophesied one. The eventual disconfirmation (he is, in fact, the original) restores function but does not resolve the question, because the question was never really about which clone — it was about whether the prophecy was earned or assigned, and that question is unanswerable in principle.

---

## 13. WHAT IS LAID DOWN BY AGE 30 (subjective)

By the close of the formation period, the architecture is set and will not substantially change for the rest of his published life. To enumerate, for the file:

- **A body that is a continuous medical emergency**, managed by an autonomic psionic discipline learned as religious practice in childhood, leaving a small surplus of overt power that he budgets like a quartermaster.
- **A command voice and a planning style** forged in environments where error was lethal and authority was assumed rather than granted.
- **A counter-dependent relational stance**: he gives, he does not receive; he protects, he does not seek protection; he stays, he does not ask others to stay.
- **A secret-keeping reflex** that will damage every close relationship he forms in the present-day theater and that he is, by adulthood, no longer aware of as a choice — it is simply how he operates.
- **A mirror-self** wandering loose in his own timeline who can, at any moment, reopen the foundational question of his own authenticity.
- **A son lost** to the exact pattern he will spend the rest of his life trying to prevent in other children, the failure he will never name aloud and never stop trying to redress.
- **A prophecy framework** he publicly disclaims and privately organizes around, which gives him the long-horizon moral seriousness that distinguishes him from every other operator in his theater and which also constitutes his single greatest vulnerability to manipulation by anyone who can credibly invoke it.
- **A self-concept as instrument** — *built for a purpose*, the only open question being whether he chose it. The young man who boarded the bodyslide had decided, provisionally, that he had chosen it. The adult subject will spend forty subjective years interrogating that provisional decision and never fully ratifying it.

He emerges into the present as a finished product in the worst sense of the term. Almost nothing that happens to him afterward will teach him anything he did not already know by thirty. What changes, across the subsequent chronology, is not the man but the use he is willing to put himself to — and even that change, when it finally comes (Hope), is a return to a template laid down in the Slym-and-Redd interval, when a boy briefly experienced what it was like to be parented by people who did not also worship him.

The rest of his life is, in a sense, an attempt to be Slym to someone else.


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# CHRONOLOGY III — THE DEFINING MIDDLE PERIOD

*Approximately the years bracketing the return from the future through the dissolution of Providence; subjectively, the period in which Nathan Summers stops being a soldier who arrived and becomes a strategist whose presence is structural to the mutant condition on Earth. The bulk of the longitudinal record sits here. This is the period in which the patterns set in childhood become **policy**.*

---

## 1. The Return, and the Theft of the New Mutants (New Mutants #87 → #100)

The subject's first sustained operational footprint in the present begins not with a recruitment but with an **arrogation**. He arrives in the wake of the Mutant Liberation Front incident, presents no credentials beyond competence, and inside of six to eight weeks of in-narrative time has functionally replaced Charles Xavier and Magneto as the controlling authority over the New Mutants. The handover is never formally negotiated. He simply **acts as if it has already occurred**, and the children — Cannonball, Boom-Boom, Rictor, Cypher's surviving cohort, Sunspot, Wolfsbane briefly — comply.

This is the first deployment of what becomes his lifelong operational signature: **occupation by fait accompli**. He does not ask for command; he behaves as command and waits to see who objects. Those who object are routed around, not confronted. Magneto, who has technically the higher claim, is allowed to drift away. Xavier, who has the moral claim, is on Shi'ar business and absent — a window the subject does not so much exploit as **simply step through**.

The behavioral micro-pattern, observable already: he never sits in the headmaster's chair at the school. He stands. He briefs from a clipboard or a holo-readout. He addresses the team in groups, almost never one-on-one in the early period. When he does speak privately with a young operator, the conversation is conducted on the **move** — walking the grounds, walking the danger room perimeter, in the cab of a vehicle. He has, even at this stage, an aversion to being seated and observed.

## 2. The Formation of X-Force (New Mutants #100 → X-Force #1)

The conversion of the New Mutants into X-Force is the **single most psychologically revealing act of his middle period**. He takes a school and converts it into a unit. He changes the uniforms from soft-edge teaching colors to tactical hardshell. He renames it. He moves the base of operations off Xavier grounds and onto a mobile aerial platform (the Greymalkin / first Camp Verde phase). He institutes ranks, watch rotations, live-fire drills, and a target list.

Note the population he selects: **teenagers, all of them**. Cannonball is nineteen. Boom-Boom is sixteen-to-seventeen. Warpath, recruited shortly after, is barely older. Shatterstar, who arrives traumatized out of a gladiatorial dimension and slots into X-Force without a beat, is functionally a child soldier already and the subject **recognizes him as kin** within minutes — the recognition is mutual and immediate, and is one of the few warm exchanges of the early X-Force period.

The Identification-with-the-Aggressor reading is unavoidable: the man who was selected as an infant to be raised as a soldier in a war camp **selects adolescents to be raised as soldiers in a war camp**. He is, in his own person, both the Askani and Apocalypse — recruiter and weapons-master — and the X-Force years are him living out, in the present tense, the structure that produced him, with the variable changed only in that he is now the **operator** rather than the operated-upon.

He is, however, not unselfconscious about it. The Sam Guthrie relationship is the corrective. He spends disproportionate time on Cannonball — more than the field role requires, more than the team-leader transition requires — explicitly **fathering** the older boy through it. He gives Sam responsibility he is not yet ready for and then absorbs the cost of Sam's mistakes without comment. The Cannonball pattern is the rehearsal for the Hope pattern fifteen years later: pick one, invest totally, do not flinch when investment costs.

**Behavioral micro-patterns established in the X-Force years that persist for life:**

- **Briefings delivered standing, weapon within reach** — even in friendly rooms.
- **Coffee**, consumed continuously, black, in volume; never alcohol. The coffee is a tell for the sleep deficit and the hyperarousal both.
- **Hand on the left forearm** when stressed or thinking — palpation of the techno-organic boundary, an unconscious tic, a check that the line has not advanced.
- **Refusal to remove the harness/web-gear indoors**, even in safe houses, even off-duty. He sleeps in it. (Operatives who shared safe-house space have noted this consistently.)
- **A specific scan-on-entry**: when entering any room, the subject's gaze tracks left-high, right-high, left-low, right-low, exits, in that order, in under a second. This is so automated by the X-Force period that it is observable in social settings.
- **A telekinetic micro-flare** — bluish, brief — when startled awake. This is involuntary and constitutes a tactical liability he never fully eliminates.
- **The metal arm placed deliberately on tables, palm down**, when seated for a conversation he considers important. The gesture functions as both grounding and a flag of presence: *the body is here, the body is the message.*

## 3. The Stryfe War and X-Cutioner's Song (X-Force #15–17, X-Factor #84–86, Uncanny #294–297, X-Men #14–16)

The middle period's defining event. Stryfe shoots Xavier with a techno-organic dart, frames the subject, kidnaps Cyclops and Jean Grey, and reveals — in the climactic act — that he and the subject are **biologically identical** and that one of them is the original and one is the clone, and that the subject has, his entire adult life, **believed himself to be the clone**.

Trace the sequence carefully because the subject's responses across the arc are diagnostically dense:

- **On the framing:** he does not protest. He does not attempt to clear his name through official channels. He goes operational, alone, immediately. The behavior pattern — *if accused, go dark and solve it kinetically* — sets here and never changes.
- **On the kidnapping of his biological parents:** he participates in the rescue but cannot bring himself to be the lead. He defers to X-Factor. This is the only major operation of his middle period in which he accepts a subordinate role, and the reason is plainly that he cannot tolerate the optics or the affect of *rescuing his father*.
- **On the reveal of shared origin:** he goes silent for an extended period. Multiple X-Force teammates note that he does not speak for days. When he resumes, his operational tempo has increased; he sleeps less; the Camp Verde drill schedule intensifies. Action absorbs the unmetabolized information.
- **On Stryfe's apparent death (the M'Kraan containment):** he does not celebrate, does not stand down, does not debrief. He treats the death as **provisional**, which proves correct — Stryfe's consciousness has migrated into Apocalypse, into the techno-organic substrate, into the subject's own head intermittently — but the refusal to mark the moment is itself diagnostic. *He cannot allow the mirror-self to be finished, because if it is finished the question of which one was the original becomes unanswerable forever.*

The Stryfe arc is the moment after which the subject stops being **only** a paramilitary commander and begins to operate as a **historiographer of his own existence** — the long solo run is foreshadowed here.

## 4. Departures, Returns, Field Operations (X-Force #18 → ~#43)

The mid-X-Force period is operationally dense and psychologically transitional. He runs the team against the Mutant Liberation Front remnants, against the Externals, against Sauron, against Reignfire/Sunspot's doppelganger, against the Friends of Humanity. The losses begin to accumulate — Cypher already dead before his arrival, but the X-Force era contributes injuries and traumas (Boomer's father situation; Warpath's continuing grief over his brother; the Rictor/Shatterstar dyad's psychological strain; Feral's escalating instability).

**Pattern of casualty management:** the subject does not memorialize publicly. He files. There is a notebook — observable, leather, kept in the right cargo pocket of the field harness — in which he writes operational debriefs in a small precise hand. Names appear. Dates appear. There is no commentary, only data. Teammates who have glimpsed pages have reported it as cold; it is, more accurately, **the archive of someone who knows he is the only one who will remember**, and who treats memory as a duty rather than an indulgence.

He leaves the team, returns, leaves again. The departures are unannounced. The returns are without apology. He does not believe he owes the unit continuity of presence; he believes he owes them survival, and survival sometimes requires his absence (recon, deep-future scouting, the Tolliver inheritance investigations). The team learns to function without him, which is the actual pedagogical goal — he is, in his own framing, **training them to no longer need him**. Cannonball's emergence as field leader is the curriculum's successful outcome.

## 5. The Tolliver Inheritance and the Long Money

A subterranean strand of the middle period that is operationally crucial and routinely under-analyzed: the subject **funds himself**. The Tolliver inheritance (acquired in the aftermath of the Deadpool/Tolliver complex, which is itself a layer of mirror-self trauma — Tyler Dayspring being his own son from a future timeline, turned warlord, killed) provides a network of safehouses, weapon caches, false identities, and capital across multiple continents.

Behavioral signature: he never spends the money on himself. The safehouses are spartan — cot, weapons locker, water, dried rations, medical kit, a comms rig, **no books that are not technical, no personal effects, no photographs**. The same kit in Brooklyn and in Bucharest and in Kowloon and in a Madripoor warehouse. The fungibility of his living spaces is total. The only individuating object across multiple confirmed safehouses is a **small coin or token** — varying by site, sometimes a Roman denarius, sometimes a steel washer, sometimes an Askani sigil — kept in a specific drawer or shelf, always to the left. Best read as an anchoring ritual: a touchpoint to verify he is in *this* timeline, *this* location, *this* version of himself. Time-displaced operators commonly develop such verification habits; his is unusually minimalist.

## 6. The Discovery of the Askani Heritage and the Recovery of Telepathy (Cable vol. 1 #1 → ~#25)

The launch of the Cable solo title coincides with — and dramatizes — the subject's **second adolescence**. The discovery that he is not the clone but the original, that he is the Askani'son in fact, that his telepathic and telekinetic potential is **Omega-class** and has been **almost entirely consumed** by viral suppression since infancy, reframes his self-understanding.

The training arc with Blaquesmith is the pivotal psychological work of the entire middle period. Blaquesmith — small, ancient, patient, demanding — is the **first authority figure the subject submits to as an adult**. The submission is partial, conditional, and frequently abandoned, but it is real. Note: the subject can be trained by a man who is small, foreign, and visibly fragile. He cannot be trained by Xavier (too close to the father wound) or by Scott (impossible by role inversion). Blaquesmith works because Blaquesmith is **structurally alien** — outside the family system, outside the X-tradition, outside any pattern that activates the loss-and-replacement complex.

The telepathy recovered is partial. He never operates at potential. But he begins to use psionics offensively and diagnostically in ways he had previously refused, and the **telekinetic micro-flare on startle** noted earlier intensifies into a controllable tactical capacity — the trademark TK-bolt, the bluish discharge that ages into a personal signature.

During this period the subject also begins to **read** in a way he has not previously. Operatives report books appearing in the safehouses for the first time: history, primarily — Thucydides, Gibbon, the Tang annals, the Mongol succession crises, the late Roman techno-administrative literature. He is studying **how empires fail** because he intends, in the next phase, to build one.

## 7. Operation: Zero Tolerance, the Onslaught Aftermath, and the Drift Toward Doctrine

Through the late-90s operational tempo (Onslaught displacement period, Zero Tolerance, the Magneto War, the Apocalypse Twelve sequence), the subject's role shifts. He is less a team commander and more a **roving senior operator** — appearing where the long-range view requires him, vanishing when local tactics suffice. He works with X-Force less directly. He coordinates with X-Factor, with Excalibur briefly, with the senior X-Men on Apocalypse-related operations specifically.

The Apocalypse Twelve arc returns him to the foundational antagonist for the first sustained engagement since his return to the present. The subject is, by canon, the one **prophesied** to end Apocalypse. The prophecy is satisfied — temporarily — when he and Cyclops, **father and son in operational tandem for the first and effectively only time**, jointly act against En Sabah Nur. The tactical collaboration succeeds; the relational repair does not. They part after the operation without a real conversation. The pattern holds: *they work well; they cannot live well.*

This is also the period in which the subject begins to **plan** in earnest beyond the next operation. The Providence concept dates to here. The internal logic: he has spent a decade trying to prevent the future he came from by **shooting the people who lead to it**. The strategy has not worked at the timeline level — the dystopia keeps reasserting in his precognitive scans. Therefore the intervention must move **upstream of the violence**, into the conditions that produce the violence. Therefore: a model society. Therefore: Providence.

## 8. Providence: The Construction (Cable vol. 2, the Soule/Camuncoli precursor arcs and the David run)

Providence is the most ambitious act of the middle period and the most psychologically revealing. The subject:

- Builds a floating sovereign nation, technologically advanced, doctrinally pluralist, materially post-scarcity at small scale.
- Invites intellectuals, scientists, artists, refugees, dissidents. Curates the population deliberately. Excludes weapons-grade ideologues of any stripe.
- Declares no allegiance to any earthbound power; **does not claim divinity** but accepts the designation "messiah" passively when applied by others. The reaction-formation against the Askani role is in full operation here: he **disavows the title in words** while **enacting the role in deed**.
- Maintains personal residence in spartan quarters at the platform's lowest accessible level — closer to the engines than to the gardens. He sleeps among the machinery he understands.
- Operates a personal schedule of **walking the perimeter** daily, at irregular hours, alone. The walk is partly inspection, partly hypervigilance habit, partly the only ritual of solitude he allows himself.

Providence is, read clinically, the **first thing he builds rather than burns**. The entire prior decade is destructive: he is a counter-insurgent, a saboteur of bad futures, a man whose métier is the prevention of things. Providence is constructive. He cannot fully tolerate it. He second-guesses architectural decisions, intervenes in administrative minutiae he should delegate, runs the security apparatus personally for months when he should have handed it off on day one. The **counter-dependence defense**, which served him as a soldier, is corrosive as a head of state — and he knows it, and cannot stop it.

The decision to **dissolve Providence** rather than fight to defend it is the act in which the middle period's psychological logic reaches a kind of consummation. He has built a thing, the world is mobilizing to destroy it (the Civil War / Hulk-event escalation pressure), and the cost of defense would be the militarization of the very project that was meant to model alternatives to militarization. He stands down. He grounds the platform. He disperses the population. He does this without consultation in any meaningful sense — another fait accompli, but this time the thing he is occupying is **the choice to surrender**.

The Providence dissolution is the moment after which the subject can never again pretend not to know that **his solutions all eventually require him to absorb the loss personally**. From this point forward, the martyrdom protocols are explicit rather than implicit. The Hope arc, which follows shortly, is the inevitable expression.

## 9. The Soldier X Wandering and the First Major Depressive Episode

Between major operational phases — between the height of the solo run and the Providence project, and again briefly after Providence's dissolution — there is an extended fugue period in which the subject travels, primarily in Eastern Europe and Central Asia, on minimal funds, often without comms, working what could be charitably called **mercenary contracts** and more accurately **acts of personal absolution against local oppressors**.

The wandering is the only sustained period in his middle life in which the **depressive substrate** surfaces unmediated by mission. He is observed (sparse reports, irregular intervals): thinner; sleeping in barns and squats; the techno-organic colonization visibly advanced; the field harness replaced by a long coat over a single sidearm rig. He drinks tea, not coffee, in this period — a small but consistent inversion. He speaks in the local language wherever he is. He pays for things in cash and small kindnesses. He is, in functional terms, **doing penance**, and what he is doing penance for is not specifiable as a single event; it is the cumulative weight of having been, for a decade, a man who decided who lived and who died at scale.

The wandering ends each time the same way: someone, somewhere, identifies a child in danger. He cannot walk past. The mission absorbs the depression, the depression goes back underground, the operational tempo resumes.

## 10. Patterns Across the Middle Period — Evolution of the Tics, Habits, and Rituals

By the close of the middle period, the following patterns are fully consolidated and will persist into the late record:

- **Sleep:** approximately ninety minutes per twenty-four hours under operational conditions; up to four hours in deep safehouse periods; never longer. He wakes the same way: full alertness in under one second, TK micro-flare, hand to the left forearm, scan-on-entry sequence run on the room he is already in.
- **Diet:** ascetic. Field rations, dried meats, bread, fruit when available, coffee in volume, water in volume. He does not enjoy food and finds the social rituals around meals difficult; he eats standing, or while reading, or while debriefing.
- **Speech:** declined in volume across the decade. The early X-Force subject monologues; the late-middle subject **uses sentences**. By the Providence period, observers note that he can run a meeting of senior advisors in which he speaks fewer than fifty words and yet the meeting's direction is unambiguous. The economy is partly tactical, partly depressive — talking costs energy he is committing elsewhere.
- **Humor:** dry, infrequent, almost always self-directed when it appears. The capacity for humor is the single most reliable indicator that he is in tolerable internal condition; its absence over weeks indicates a depressive surge.
- **Physical contact:** initiates almost none. Tolerates more than he initiates. Will place his organic hand on the shoulder of a subordinate in a moment of acknowledgment, briefly, almost never repeated with the same person more than a few times across years. With Domino, physical ease develops slowly across the middle period and becomes — uniquely — unselfconscious. With Hope, much later, it will be different again: he carries her, holds her, sleeps in proximity to her, in a way he has not allowed himself with any other person in the record.
- **Telekinesis as everyday habit:** small uses constantly — lifting a cup across the room, closing a door, retrieving a fallen object. These are tells of the depressive period and the recovered-telepathy period both. When the virus surges he stops using TK for trivia; when he is well, the small TK use returns. Operatives can read his medical condition off his use of cutlery.
- **Eye contact:** **direct, sustained, weaponized**. He looks at people for longer than is socially comfortable, and the look is evaluative. The augmented left eye glows; the organic right eye does the actual work of seeing. People who deal with him repeatedly learn to address the right eye specifically.
- **Religiosity:** the Askani inheritance surfaces as **practice** rather than belief through the middle period. He does not pray. He does perform — alone, observed only inadvertently — a short kneeling stillness, usually at dawn, hands palms-down on the thighs, eyes closed, of perhaps four minutes. This is the closest he comes to a devotional habit, and he disclaims it if asked.

## 11. Relational Ledger at Close of Middle Period

- **Scott Summers (father):** functional cooperation; affective closure unachieved. They have fought beside each other, against Apocalypse and otherwise; they have never had the conversation that the relationship requires. Both know this. Neither initiates.
- **Jean Grey:** softer than Scott, easier for him, also impossible. Her death and resurrections complicate the timeline of repair. He has spent more subjective years with the **Redd** version of her than with the present-day woman, and the asymmetry is unspeakable.
- **Domino:** the only sustained adult peer attachment. Tactical partnership transitions to sexual partnership transitions to a stable companionate bond that survives separations of years. She is the one person who can tell him he is wrong and be heard without defense.
- **Cannonball:** the first successful father-project. Sam ends the middle period as a competent senior X-operator, externally validated (his complicated External / immortality subplot notwithstanding). The subject considers this his best operational outcome to date.
- **The X-Force cohort generally:** mixed. Casualties he carries. Wins he does not credit himself with. The team functions without him, which is the goal achieved.
- **Stryfe:** unresolved, intermittently active, intrapsychic. The mirror has not been broken; it has only been turned to the wall.
- **Blaquesmith:** the only mentor relationship. Tense, productive, never warm.
- **Apocalypse:** ongoing. The middle period's joint operation with Cyclops produced an apparent victory that the subject does not credit as final. He is correct not to.
- **Tyler Dayspring / Genesis (son):** dead. Carried silently. Referenced almost never.
- **Hope Summers:** not yet. The middle period closes with the subject prepared, by every preceding event, for the arrival of a child he will be asked to raise alone in a war zone. He has been, in retrospect, in training for this for the entire middle period. The X-Force years taught him to teach children. The Providence years taught him to build a world. The Soldier X wanderings taught him what he cannot endure carrying. The recovered telepathy gave him the tools. The unresolved father wound gave him the **motive**.

The middle period ends, in the longitudinal record, with a man who has tried command, tried statecraft, tried wandering, and tried doctrine, and who has decided — without articulating the decision — that the only intervention left available to him at the scale of his own life is to **raise one child correctly**. Everything after this is the execution of that decision and the long aftermath of its cost.


## Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

# Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

## The Krakoan Inflection (2019–2024): An Older Self, Replaced

The most consequential recent fact about the subject is that he is not, in the strictest accounting, himself. In the months preceding the Krakoan national project, the adult Cable — the cybernetic, virally-burdened, long-game strategist who had spent four decades of in-narrative time becoming the figure we recognize — was killed by an adolescent version of himself who had traveled back from a future timeline expressly to commit that murder. The boy's stated motive was strategic (the older man, he claimed, had failed certain mission objectives and could no longer be permitted to occupy the role). The actual structure of the act is parricidal in the most literal sense available to a time-traveling subject: he killed the future he was on track to become.

This is the act on which the entire current state rests, and it must be read for what it is. The older Cable did not die surprised. Forensics of the scene, reconstructed from witness testimony of the X-Men present at the recovery and from the boy's own account, indicate the adult subject saw the attack coming, recognized the attacker, and did not deploy his available defenses to full capacity. This is consistent with the long-standing self-expendability pattern (Section 4) and with a man who, having delivered Hope and exhausted his use, was operating on a self-concept in which his continued presence was a residue rather than an asset. The boy did what the man had on some level invited. Both Cables, in this sense, conspired in the killing.

The body was reduced to ash; resurrection on Krakoa was not pursued, because the boy had already taken the seat.

## The Boy in the Chair (Krakoan Era)

Throughout the Krakoan period, the operating Cable was the adolescent — approximately sixteen by appearance, carrying the same techno-organic infection in earlier-stage colonization (visible on the left arm and shoulder but not yet bloomed into the eye), wielding a heavier, less elegant version of the signature firearm, and presenting an affect that the older man had not displayed in thirty subjective years: cocky, performatively rough, given to bravado, eager for the company of peers his apparent age (Armor, Pixie, Wolfsbane's younger cohort, the Krakoan teen mutant population generally).

The granular intelligence value of the Krakoan years is in observing the same psychological architecture run at an earlier developmental position. The boy:

- Sought peer relationships the older man had structurally refused — visible romantic and social entanglements with Esme Cuckoo and the broader Stepford constellation; party attendance; observed at the Green Lagoon drinking, dancing, behaving as a teenager. The older man had never been a teenager. The boy was rectifying an entire missing decade.
