- **Right thumb still:** stress onset.
- **Left eye blink-lag extending past 150 ms:** elevated arousal.
- **Left eye stops blinking entirely:** combat state.
- **Latency to respond drops to zero:** under load.
- **Voice volume drops:** stress or grief.
- **Voice volume rises:** performance, not affect.
- **Hand braces against vertical surface:** acute grief moment.
- **Removes left glove without comment:** trust extension.
- **Half-second tilt of head downward, full body stillness:** telepathic engagement or prayer (the postures are identical).
- **Disappears within twenty minutes of victory:** normal.
- **Walks for days or months after defeat:** also normal.
- **Compliments by name and specific action, exact


## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# UATU DOSSIER — NATHAN CHRISTOPHER CHARLES SUMMERS
## Section 7: Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

> *"A week of observation. Treat every hour as data. Note the silences as carefully as the actions — with this subject the silences are the action."*

---

## 7.0 Framing Note

The subject does not have a "daily life" in the civilian sense. He has an **operational tempo** that he reverts to under any conditions short of medical collapse, and that tempo is structurally identical whether the location is a Manhattan safehouse, the bridge of a floating sovereign nation, a cave in post-apocalyptic 4500 AD, or the converted root system of a Krakoan habitation cylinder. The constants travel with him. Strip away geography, era, and current alias and what remains is a small set of repeated, almost liturgical actions performed in the same order at the same biological cues for the better part of seven decades of subjective lived time. This file documents those constants.

A full surveillance week would record, at most, **eleven to fourteen hours of behavior the subject himself would consider "his own."** The remainder is mission, maintenance, and the continuous internal labor of holding the techno-organic infection at the cellular boundary. The reader should understand that the subject is, in clinical terms, *never off-shift*. There is no domestic Nathan. There is the operator at varying intensities.

---

## 7.1 The 24-Hour Cycle

### 7.1.1 Pre-dawn (approx. 03:30–05:00, local)

The subject wakes without alarm. He has not used an alarm since the Askani war camps; the body has been conditioned to a roughly 90-minute polyphasic pattern with one main sleep block of three to four hours ending before astronomical twilight. He surfaces flat — no stretching, no orienting fog, eyes open and tracking the room before any limb has moved. Surveillance footage from the Providence years (recovered in fragments after the station's surrender) shows this consistently: **the head turns first, then the left hand goes to the bedside weapon**, then the body sits up as a single hinged motion. Total elapsed time from REM termination to combat-ready: under four seconds. He has performed this sequence so many times it no longer registers as vigilance to him; it is simply how a body wakes.

The first deliberate act of the day is **a forty-to-ninety-second internal scan** — a learned psionic ritual in which he audits the perimeter of the T-O infection, identifies any overnight territorial gain, and re-establishes the telekinetic seal at the cellular border. To an observer this presents as the subject sitting on the edge of the bed, left forearm resting palm-up on the left knee, head slightly bowed, completely motionless, the left eye dimmed nearly to off. It looks like prayer. Functionally it *is* prayer, in a very narrow sense: it is the daily renewal of a covenant he made with his own dying flesh, that he will not let it finish what it started.

After the scan: water. Plain, room temperature, between 500 and 750 ml, drunk in one continuous draw without setting the glass down. He does not eat for at least ninety minutes after waking. Coffee, when available, comes second — black, no sugar, brewed strong enough to be a stimulant rather than a beverage. In environments without coffee he substitutes whatever local equivalent exists (Krakoan biotic infusion; Askani-era stim cubes; instant powder in field conditions) with no detectable change in mood. He is not a connoisseur of anything. He is a man dosing himself.

### 7.1.2 First movement block (approx. 04:00–05:30)

A physical regimen, performed in whatever clear floor space exists, **without music, without mirrors, and without varying the order**. The sequence has been stable since at least the early X-Force period and is observable across thirty in-canon years:

1. **Mobility / joint articulation** — twelve to fifteen minutes, low-amplitude, focused disproportionately on the left shoulder, left hip, and cervical spine, which are the regions most colonized by T-O hardware and most prone to mechanical seizure overnight.
2. **Calisthenic strength loop** — push, pull, hinge, squat, carry, performed for time rather than reps; he does not count. The left arm load is asymmetric to compensate for the prosthetic-functional limb's different recruitment pattern.
3. **Weapons handling without firing** — draw, present, dry-fire, reholster; blade transitions; reload drills with whatever sidearm is current. Performed eyes-closed at least once per session. This is muscle-memory maintenance, not training; he treats it the way a pianist treats scales.
4. **Cooldown stand** — three to seven minutes of standing perfectly still, eyes open, breathing nasally, performing a second perimeter scan of the infection. Observers have repeatedly mistaken this for him "spacing out." He is doing the opposite of spacing out.

Total block: 60–90 minutes. He does not skip it. He has performed an abbreviated version of it while concussed, while shot, and on at least one documented occasion while bleeding heavily from a flank wound he had not yet bothered to dress.

### 7.1.3 Morning intelligence intake (approx. 05:30–07:00)

This is the most consistent block across the subject's life and the one a surveillance operation would learn to set its watch by. He gathers, in order:

- **Local situational awareness** — weather, traffic patterns visible from his current window, civilian foot density, anything out of baseline for the location. Performed by standing at the window for several minutes, drinking the second water of the day, *not moving the curtain*. He stands offset from the frame.
- **Threat board review** — current operational dossiers, news of mutant affairs, geopolitical instability, anything Apocalypse-adjacent, anything child-soldier-adjacent. In modern eras this is digital; in lower-tech contexts he reads physical print or briefs from couriers. He prefers redundancy: at least two sources for any given fact before he weights it.
- **Timeline check** — a private, idiosyncratic practice. He keeps a running mental ledger of which near-future events he has either witnessed personally, been briefed on by future allies, or extrapolated. Each morning he updates the ledger against the day's news and notes divergences. Surveillance has occasionally captured him **writing short alphanumeric strings in a small notebook** that no cryptanalyst has decoded; the working theory is that these are dated tags for branched probability outcomes, indexed to his own memory. He carries this notebook on his person at all times. He has burned at least three of them on operational compromise.

### 7.1.4 First meal (approx. 07:00–07:30)

Eaten standing or in a posture that permits immediate departure. Composition: high protein, high fat, moderate complex carbohydrate, deliberately bland. The T-O virus increases caloric demand by an estimated 40–60% over baseline for a man his size; he eats accordingly and without ceremony. Documented preferences when nothing operational pressures the menu: **eggs, oats, dense bread, cured meat, hard cheese, fruit if seasonal**. He drinks more water with the meal. He does not read or speak during eating; the meal is itself a task and he wants it complete.

He is observed, across all eras, to **finish his plate**. This is a war-camp child's habit and it has never left him.

### 7.1.5 Working day (variable; nominally 07:30–19:00)

The shape of the working day depends entirely on whether the subject is in **command, operation, or transit** mode.

- **In command mode** (Providence period; X-Force founding period; Krakoan committee participation), the subject conducts briefings standing, prefers small group sizes (three to five), runs meetings on hard time limits, and ends them when the decision is made rather than when the agenda is exhausted. He does not socialize before or after. He delivers orders in declarative sentences, repeats them once for confirmation, and walks out. Surveillance of Providence's bridge logs shows his average verbal contribution per meeting was under 90 seconds; he listened more than he spoke and spoke only to decide.
- **In operation mode** (active mission), the day collapses into the mission's tempo. He eats on the move, sleeps in 20–40 minute fragments, and does not perform the morning movement block — he substitutes a compressed three-minute mobility sequence done in whatever cover is available.
- **In transit mode** (between missions, or wandering periods such as Soldier X), the day extends into long, slow, deliberate movement through a landscape, broken by intelligence-gathering stops. He walks more than he drives. He prefers trains to planes. In Eastern Europe during the depowered period he is reliably documented to have walked **fifteen to twenty-five kilometers per day** for weeks at a stretch, sleeping in barns, monasteries, and abandoned Soviet infrastructure.

### 7.1.6 Midday and afternoon

He does not take lunch as a discrete event. Food is grazed — hard rations, jerky, dried fruit, bread — from a pouch he keeps on his person, every two to three hours, in small quantities. This is consistent with a body running a continuous metabolic deficit against the virus and a personality that refuses to designate any portion of the day as recreational.

The afternoon typically contains the day's **second intelligence pass** (lighter than the morning's) and any planned interpersonal contact. Subject batches interpersonal contact deliberately, the way a person with low social tolerance might batch errands. He will see allies, debrief contacts, or hold a conversation with a recruit, then return to solitude for hours.

### 7.1.7 Evening (approx. 19:00–22:00)

The evening is the only portion of the day that occasionally resembles civilian life, and even then thinly. He eats a second real meal — the largest of the day, calorie-dense — and afterward, in stable periods, will permit himself one of a small inventory of low-stimulus solitary activities:

- **Reading**, almost exclusively non-fiction: military history (he reads campaigns the way other people read novels, looking for the decision point), strategic theory, agricultural manuals (a habit from Providence), and — a tell — **child development literature** acquired quietly during the Hope years and never disposed of.
- **Equipment maintenance**, performed at a table with a cloth laid down, in the same order each time: sidearms first, blades second, then prosthetics interface check, then optics. He cleans weapons he has not fired that day. He cleans them roughly twice as often as is mechanically necessary. The activity is regulatory; it is how he comes down from the day.
- **Correspondence**, when relationships are active. He writes short letters in a precise, all-caps block hand (a habit acquired from military report writing and never abandoned). Letters to Hope during separations are documented to run two to four pages, always factual on the surface, always emotionally readable underneath if one knows him. He does not keep copies.

He does not watch entertainment. He does not listen to music for pleasure; he listens to it for environmental scan (lyrics for local mood, instrumentation for cultural cues). He does not drink. He does not smoke, despite the period iconography of the cigar — that was a costume he wore briefly in early X-Force days and discarded once he stopped needing to perform "hardened soldier" for his own recruits' benefit.

### 7.1.8 Late evening rituals (approx. 22:00–23:30)

A second, longer **internal scan** — this one running closer to three minutes, more thorough, calibrated to set the cellular boundary for the long sleep block. He performs it sitting cross-legged on the floor, never on the bed. The bed is for sleep; the floor is for work. He maintains this distinction obsessively.

Following the scan: a **perimeter walk** of whatever space he is in. Doors, windows, exits, sightlines, the position of any other occupants. In safe environments this is a thirty-second formality. In contested environments it can extend to twenty minutes and include placement of small passive alarms (a cup balanced on a doorknob in low-tech settings; trip sensors in high). He has been observed to perform the perimeter walk in environments he has slept in safely for months. He cannot not do it.

He then **lays out the next day's equipment**, in the same arrangement each time: clothing folded in the order it will be donned, weapons within left-hand reach, boots placed parallel and oriented for immediate step-in. This is a war-camp habit. It is also, by his own oblique admission in one early-2000s solo issue, a hedge against the possibility of dying in his sleep — he wants whoever finds the body to find it in a room that looks like the room of a soldier, not a man caught off-guard.

### 7.1.9 Sleep (approx. 23:30–03:30, plus fragmentary naps)

Four hours, on average, in the main block. Supplemented by one or two **micro-sleeps** of 15–25 minutes taken during the day when conditions permit, performed sitting upright with the back against a wall and the dominant hand free. He has done this so consistently that the body now refuses prone sleep outside of stable environments; he reports (to Domino, on record) that lying flat in unfamiliar places feels "wrong in the spine."

Dreams are not discussed. The few canonical glimpses suggest a recurring intrusion set: the Askani war camp, his mother (which mother is variable), Stryfe's face, and a child he failed to reach in time. He wakes from the worst of these silently, without thrashing, and goes directly into the morning movement block regardless of clock time. A surveillance team would record nights on which the subject is up at 01:30 doing slow controlled push-ups in the dark and would have no immediate way of knowing those are the bad nights.

---

## 7.2 Habits of Place

The subject's relationship to *space* is as patterned as his relationship to time.

**Seat selection:** Back to a wall, eyes to the door, with a secondary line of sight to at least one window. He will rearrange the furniture of a borrowed room within his first hour of occupancy to enable this. He has done so in conference rooms, restaurants, and on at least one occasion a hospital recovery suite, getting out of bed to move a chair before consenting to be examined.

**Doorways:** He pauses, briefly, before crossing any unfamiliar threshold. The pause is under a second and most observers miss it. It is a sightline check.

**Vehicles:** Prefers to drive. When a passenger, sits in the position that affords fastest egress (front right in left-drive jurisdictions; rear-left-diagonal in taxis). On aircraft, aisle seat near an exit, every time.

**Cities he revisits:** He maintains a small mental map of safe rooms, weapons caches, sympathetic contacts, and reliable couriers in roughly two dozen urban centers across multiple eras. Surveillance would observe him **rotating** through these caches on a quarterly basis when in a given region, replacing perishables (ammunition primers, medical supplies, currency), without ever using them. Caches are emergency infrastructure; using one would mean something had gone very wrong.

**Wild and rural environments:** Markedly more relaxed than in urban. Shoulders drop, gait lengthens, scanning frequency reduces. This is consistent with a childhood spent in landscapes without dense crowds; the city is the unfamiliar environment for him, not the wilderness.

**The Providence period specifically:** When he had a place that was *his*, he organized it like a working farm with a command post inside it. He gardened. The agricultural manuals on his current shelves are residue of that period. He has not had a place of his own since, and there is reason to read the loss of Providence as a wound he has not fully metabolized — the only home he ever built as an adult, surrendered to prevent escalation. He does not speak of it. He keeps seeds, though. In a small tin. They are old now.

---

## 7.3 Weekly and Seasonal Rhythms

A surveillance week — say, a notional seven-day observation window in a stable mid-career period — would yield the following macropattern:

- **Daily constants:** wake / scan / movement block / intake / meal / work / batched social / equipment maintenance / scan / perimeter / sleep.
- **One full ammunition and equipment audit per week**, usually performed Saturday or its local equivalent, taking three to five hours. He uses a written checklist that he then destroys.
- **One longer physical session per week** — a sustained ruck, swim, or terrain run of three to six hours, performed alone, treated as a stress test of both body and viral containment under exertion.
- **One scheduled contact with an external ally** — historically Domino, more recently Hope, occasionally Scott. Conducted by secure means, kept short, content largely operational with personal substrate readable only between lines.
- **One unscheduled disappearance every few weeks**, lasting hours to days, with no explanation offered or expected. Working hypothesis: these correspond to flare-ups of the infection requiring isolation, or to private timeline reconnaissance, or both.

Seasonally, he is observed to **withdraw further in winter**, eat more, sleep marginally longer, and reduce social contact to operational minimums. This pattern dates from the Soldier X period and has not remitted. It is consistent with a low-grade seasonal worsening of the underlying dysthymic process.

Anniversaries register, though he denies them. The dates of Hope's birth, of his own delivery to the Askani, of the loss of Providence, and of Stryfe's first death are observable as nights on which the subject does not sleep at all and the morning movement block runs long. He would never name them. The body keeps the calendar he refuses to.

---

## 7.4 The Tells

A surveillance officer trained on the subject would learn to read the following micro-signals:

- **The left hand at the left temple.** A brief, almost incidental gesture, fingertips to the side of the skull above the cybernetic eye. Occurs when the T-O is pressing. Frequency of the gesture is a real-time readout of his viral load. Six times an hour is baseline. Twenty times an hour means he is in trouble and will not say so.
- **The pause before speaking.** Half a second longer than conversational norm. He is running the sentence through a tactical filter before releasing it. The pause disappears entirely with people he fully trusts (a population of perhaps four) and lengthens with people he is managing.
- **The flat right palm on a flat surface.** Hand laid down, fingers spread, pressed gently. A self-grounding behavior, observed in moments of suppressed emotion. Catalogued at: the news of Hope's safety after Second Coming; the first encounter with the adolescent version of himself; standing over the wreckage of Providence.
- **The thousand-yard look that is not actually a thousand yards.** When his gaze unfocuses, he is not dissociating — he is timeline-checking, running a probability tree against current inputs. Duration: one to four seconds. He returns to the conversation having missed nothing.
- **The single nod.** His standard acknowledgment, used in place of "yes," "understood," "I agree," and "I love you." Context disambiguates. Recipients who have known him long enough learn the difference.
- **The refusal of offered comfort.** Food pressed on him, a chair offered, a blanket, a hand on the shoulder — declined with a small headshake and no eye contact. He cannot accept caretaking. It does not parse as kindness; it parses as the prelude to being left.

---

## 7.5 What a Week of Surveillance Would Conclude

That the subject lives a life of **extraordinary internal discipline organized around the suppression of a single terminal fact**: that his body has been trying to kill him since infancy and only continuous, conscious labor prevents it. Every routine documented above is, at root, a function of that fact. The pre-dawn wake is because a sick body sleeps lightly. The internal scans are the literal medical regimen. The hypervigilance, the cached supplies, the perimeter walks, the refusal of comfort, the asymmetric musculature, the rationed food, the militarized sleep — all of it serves the same project, which is to remain operational for one more day.

The remainder — the gardening seeds in the tin, the child development books on the shelf, the letters to Hope in block capitals, the nights he does not sleep on dates he will not name — is the residue of the man underneath the project. There is not much of it, and he guards it more carefully than he guards the weapons. But it is there. A patient surveillance file, kept long enough, would document it.

He would, of course, know he was being watched the entire time. He always does. The fact that the file exists at all means he has, on some level, permitted it.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

*Subject: Nathan Christopher Charles Summers. Relational substrate: a family tree that branches across two millennia, three timelines, and at least four clones. Every primary attachment in this section is contaminated by time-displacement, identity duplication, or both. The subject's adult relational pathology cannot be understood without the topology of this map.*

---

## 5.1 — SCOTT SUMMERS (FATHER)

**Status:** Living (variable, given comic mortality). Relationship: chronically unresolved, structurally unrepairable.

**Origin of the bond:** Scott Summers held his infant son for a matter of weeks before surrendering him to Sister Askani in the Inferno aftermath (*X-Factor #68*). The handover is the founding wound. Scott's reasoning was sound — the techno-organic virus was unsurvivable in present-day medicine; the future offered the only chance. Nathan, the infant, did not have access to that reasoning. He had access only to the somatic memory of his father's arms opening. This is the first abandonment, and every subsequent relationship Cable has with an authority figure is filtered through it.

**The Slym/Redd interregnum:** Scott and Jean lived twelve years in the future as Nathan's adoptive parents under assumed identities, raising him through the precognitive intervention of Rachel Summers' Askani consciousness (*Adventures of Cyclops and Phoenix*). This is the single happiest period of Nathan's life on the record, and it is constructed entirely on a lie of identity. He believed Slym and Redd were strangers who loved him. They were his actual parents, wearing the disguise of strangers, who would be ripped out of his life on a fixed timetable they could not disclose. When they vanished, the child experienced it as a third abandonment, not a temporal recall. The recursive cruelty of this — that his parents *did* raise him, lovingly, and he can never get that childhood back as a clean memory, because the people in it were lying about who they were — is one of the engines of his adult emotional flatness. He cannot trust warmth that arrives wearing a face.

**Adult re-encounter:** When Cable returns to the present, he is biologically older than Scott. This is not a curable asymmetry. Scott cannot occupy the father role because he is younger, less experienced, and has not lived through his son's war. Cable cannot occupy the son role because he carries memories of raising himself in conditions Scott has only read about. Both men respond to the impossibility with extreme professionalism. They work well together tactically — see *Messiah Complex*, *Second Coming*, the Krakoa-era operations — and almost never speak as family. The relationship has been displaced into operational register because the familial register is foreclosed.

**Trust / Safety:** Tactical trust is total — Scott is the one X-Man Cable will follow without backbrief. Emotional safety is near zero, in both directions. Neither has ever wept in the other's presence on the record. They communicate love through tactical deference: Cable accepts Scott's plans even when he disagrees, and Scott has, twice (Messiah Complex, Second Coming), entrusted Cable with the survival of the mutant species. That is how this family says *I love you*.

**Failure mode:** When pressed to discuss the original surrender, both men retreat into mission language. Cable has, on record, told Scott some variant of *you did what you had to* — which is technically forgiveness and functionally a foreclosure. The conversation that would actually heal the bond cannot occur because it would require Cable to admit it hurt, and he does not have the affective vocabulary for that admission with a man who is also his commander.

**Leverage:** Anyone wishing to move Cable through Scott has a narrow window: Scott in mortal danger produces in Cable a regression to the abandoned infant, manifesting as catastrophically high risk tolerance. Conversely, Scott asking Cable to *stand down* lands harder than Scott asking him to fight; it activates the abandonment circuit (*you do not want me here*) and Cable will not comply.

---

## 5.2 — JEAN GREY / MADELYNE PRYOR (MOTHER[S])

**Status:** Both periodically dead, periodically resurrected. Bond: split at the cellular level into two women who are the same woman, neither of whom can be a mother to him cleanly.

**Madelyne Pryor — biological mother, clone, engineered womb:** Cable's mother of record was Jean Grey's clone, manufactured by Sinister for breeding purposes, married to Scott Summers under false pretenses, abandoned by him when the original returned. By the time Cable is old enough to understand his origin, Madelyne is dead (Goblin Queen arc, *Inferno*) and discredited as a person — remembered chiefly as villainess. Cable has had to construct his relationship to his actual biological mother through historical reconstruction. He has met her resurrected variants. There is no sustained mother-son dynamic on the record because Madelyne, when alive again, has typically been hostile to the Summers line or working her own agenda. He does not get to grieve her cleanly; she keeps coming back as a stranger.

**Jean Grey — adoptive mother in the future (as Redd), genetic donor, never present in the present:** Jean raised him for twelve years as Redd. After her recall, every subsequent Jean Cable meets is, from her perspective, a woman who has not raised him. He carries memories she does not have. This is the inverse of the Scott problem and somehow worse: with Scott the asymmetry is age; with Jean the asymmetry is that *only one of them remembers the relationship*. Jean has the Redd memories somewhere in her vast psionic substrate, but they are not foregrounded, and the warmth Cable received from her in the future cannot be reciprocated by the woman in the present who has, for her, just met him.

**Observed pattern:** Cable does not seek Jean out. He will not initiate. When they encounter each other, his behavior is unusually formal — *ma'am* registers, courtesies, physical distance maintained beyond his already-distant baseline. This is not coldness. It is the protective procedure of a man who cannot allow himself to expect from her what she does not remember owing.

**Trust / Safety:** Tactical trust high. Emotional safety paradoxical — she is the woman who held him as a child but the woman who does not remember holding him as a child. He cannot rest in her presence; the cognitive dissonance is too taxing.

**Failure mode:** If Jean ever fully integrated the Redd memories and presented herself to him as *mother*, present tense — the file predicts brief catastrophic affective breakthrough followed by withdrawal. He is not equipped to receive that reparation. The reparative bond he has accepted is the one *he* gives (to Hope), not the one given to him.

**Leverage:** Low. Threats to Jean produce a response, but it is filtered through the Scott channel — he protects her *as Scott's wife* more than *as his mother*, because the latter framing is more painful than he can sustain in tactical conditions.

---

## 5.3 — STRYFE (CLONE-BROTHER / NEMESIS-SELF)

**Status:** Dead in body, persistent as psychic residue. Functionally: never absent.

**Origin:** Apocalypse, anticipating that Nathan would be rescued from the future, kidnapped the infant before the Askani could fully secure him and grew a clone — Stryfe — to be raised as his heir-apparent. The clone was groomed to inherit the empire. The original was hidden, raised by the resistance, and grew up believing he was the lesser, the spare, possibly even the duplicate. *X-Cutioner's Song* reveals that Cable had operated for years under the assumption that he, not Stryfe, was the clone. Discovering otherwise did not relieve him; it deepened the wound, because the question *which of us is the real one* is not biological — it is moral, and biology does not answer it.

**Dynamics:** Stryfe is everything Cable would be if Cable's defenses had failed in the other direction. Same genome, same childhood war, same messianic framing — different outcome. Stryfe surrendered to the messianic identity and became a tyrant; Cable resisted it and became a soldier-strategist. Cable cannot kill Stryfe without killing the question *am I only different from him by an act of will, and could I lose that will?* This is why the feud is interminable. Even after Stryfe's physical death, his consciousness has inhabited Cable, possessed others, fragmented across timelines. Cable's psyche maintains a Stryfe-shaped chamber whether or not Stryfe is currently in it.

**Behavioral tell:** When Cable is interrogated about whether a given lethal decision was *necessary*, his defense is uncharacteristically vehement. He needs the moral distinction between himself and Stryfe to be load-bearing. Suggest the distinction is fragile and you will see the closest thing to unguarded anger he displays.

**Trust / Safety:** None. Stryfe is the only person from whom Cable cannot mentally distance — they share a psionic register at the genetic level, and Stryfe can find him anywhere.

**Failure mode:** Cable will absorb tactical losses rather than allow Stryfe an aesthetic victory. He will not let Stryfe make a point at his expense, which means a sufficiently clever Stryfe-aligned adversary can bait him into suboptimal engagements by framing them as Stryfe-coded.

**Leverage:** Maximum. Anyone wishing to destabilize Cable, mention Stryfe. Anyone wishing to compel a specific decision, frame the alternative as *what Stryfe would do*.

---

## 5.4 — HOPE SUMMERS (ADOPTIVE DAUGHTER)

**Status:** Living. The relationship that redeems the subject and the relationship that proves the subject is capable of redemption.

**Origin:** The first mutant born after M-Day, the messianic child of *Messiah Complex*, entrusted to Cable in the chaos of her birth and immediately hunted across every available timeline. Cable took her to the future and raised her alone for approximately sixteen subjective years (*Cable* vol. 2, 2008–2010), on the run, with a body in terminal decline.

**Dynamics — the diagnostic centerpiece:** Cable, who was raised in a future war zone by surrogates who lost him, intentionally chose to raise his daughter in a future war zone — *but stayed*. Every variable of his own childhood was reproduced (displaced time, dystopian setting, surrogate parent, identity as messianic figure, ambient warfare) except the one that mattered: the parent did not leave. This is reparative parenting executed with the precision of a man who has analyzed exactly what was done wrong to him and is determined to invert it. The success of the inversion is measurable. Hope emerged from sixteen years of apocalyptic itinerancy as a functional, attached, morally serious adult capable of trust and love. By the only metric Cable respects — the outcome for the person in his care — he is the most successful father in his bloodline.

**Specific paternal habits with Hope (drawn from the run):** Taught her firearms early (his solution to her safety was to make her dangerous, which is the only solution he believes in); taught her field medicine; read to her from salvaged books; allowed her to see him weakened by the virus when concealment became impossible, because he prioritized her ability to handle reality over the preservation of his authority; never lied to her about the stakes; never told her she was the chosen one in his own voice — let her find that frame for herself if she wanted it. The last detail is the most telling. He had the prophecy weaponized on him as a child. He refused to weaponize it on her.

**Trust / Safety:** Total, bidirectional. Hope is the only person who can ask Cable to rest and have him comply — because she is the only person whose request to rest he does not read as a prelude to abandonment. She *stayed* with him through the dying, which gives her a credit line no one else holds.

**Failure mode:** Cable will die for Hope and has. He does not have a calibration mechanism that prevents him from making her the variable around which his entire survival logic collapses. If Hope were ever turned, manipulated, or used as a bait — Cable would not survive the engagement, by intent. This is the only relationship where his self-expendability is fully unbridled.

**Leverage:** Maximum and unambiguous. Hope's safety is the move. Every actor in the relevant intelligence space knows this; the constraint is that harming her produces a Cable who will burn the architecture of reality to retrieve her, which is a deterrent of its own.

---

## 5.5 — RACHEL SUMMERS (HALF-SISTER / ASKANI FOUNDER / TEMPORAL AUNT)

**Status:** Living, time-displaced, recurring.

**Origin:** Rachel is Scott and Jean's daughter from an alternate timeline (Days of Future Past adjacent), who became Mother Askani, founded the Clan Askani in the future, and was the prime mover behind Nathan's rescue and rearing. She is, in a strict relational sense, his half-sister (from another timeline's version of his parents); in a functional sense, his great-aunt-cult-leader-grandmother. The Marvel family tree is a knot and this is the central knot.

**Dynamics:** Rachel is the architect of Nathan's survival. Without her intervention as Mother Askani, the infant dies of the T-O virus in the present. Without her clan, no one raises him. Without her bringing Scott and Jean forward, no Slym and Redd. She is, more than anyone, the *cause* of Cable's life. He owes her his existence in a way that does not admit casual acknowledgment.

**Their adult relationship is, accordingly, awkward.** They love each other with the displaced affection of family members who share too much improbable history to discuss it casually and not enough recent history to discuss it intimately. They tend to interact in the operational register, with brief flashes of warmth that neither pursues. Rachel has tried, more than Cable; he tolerates the effort and does not return it at her tempo.

**Trust / Safety:** Trust high. Safety moderate — Rachel knows things about him (his entire prophetic provenance) that he is not comfortable being known. Her continued existence is a continued evidentiary record of his constructed nature.

**Failure mode:** Cable will not refuse a Rachel-issued request that invokes the Askani framework. The conditioning is too deep. This is exploitable by anyone who can plausibly speak in that register.

**Leverage:** Moderate. Rachel in danger triggers a familial protocol that is muted compared to the Hope or Scott response but is genuine.

---

## 5.6 — NATE GREY / X-MAN (ALTERNATE-TIMELINE DOUBLE)

**Status:** Living, variably hostile, variably allied.

**Origin:** The Age of Apocalypse's Nate Grey is the unbroken, virus-free, psionically unconstrained version of what Nathan Summers would have been without Sinister's containment and Apocalypse's infection. He is the road not taken — Cable's full power realized in a body that does not have to spend it staying alive.

**Dynamics:** They are not enemies and not allies; they are the same person in different sleeves, and they treat each other with the cautious courtesy of men who recognize the resemblance and refuse to be reduced to it. Nate Grey's existence is a permanent rebuke to Cable's body — *this is what you could do if you weren't dying*. Cable's existence is a permanent rebuke to Nate Grey's discipline — *this is what restraint and decades look like*.

**Trust / Safety:** Limited. Cable does not extend full trust because Nate Grey has, across various stories, slid toward grandiosity in ways that confirm Cable's worst suspicions about what he himself would become without the containing pressure of the virus.

**Failure mode:** Cable cannot bring himself to terminate Nate Grey even when tactically warranted. To do so would be a form of self-execution. He will incapacitate, contain, redirect — never finish.

**Leverage:** Low. Few actors can credibly threaten Nate Grey to coerce Cable, but the mirror-self can be used to bait Cable into philosophical engagements that distract him from operational priorities.

---

## 5.7 — KID CABLE / ADOLESCENT SELF (*EXTERMINATION* PERIOD)

**Status:** Currently the operative Cable in some continuities; the adult having been murdered by his younger self.

**Origin:** In *Extermination*, an adolescent version of Nathan Summers — extracted from an earlier point in his timeline — assassinates his adult self for what he perceives as the adult's failure to complete the mission Hope represented. The adolescent then assumes the operational role.

**Dynamics — the most important fact in this dossier on this point:** The adult Cable, on some level, *allowed it*. Multiple readings of his combat behavior in that sequence indicate suboptimal defense. The man who has survived Apocalypse, Stryfe, and a terminal nanotech infection for sixty subjective years did not survive his own teenage self at peak effort. The dossier reads this as suicide-by-proxy executed through the only person Cable could not bring himself to fully resist — the version of himself who had not yet failed.

**The adolescent now carries the burden** of being the version of himself his adult self could not stand to be. He is working through the same patterns from an earlier developmental position, which means: same defenses, less ballast; same drives, less calibration; same self-expendability, more lethality. The Krakoan-era Cable is a younger man trying to outrun the realization that he is going to become the man he killed.

**Trust / Safety:** Not applicable in the conventional frame — the relationship is intrapersonal across time. Adolescent Cable's relationship to his adult predecessor is one of guilt-fueled overcompensation.

**Failure mode:** The adolescent will repeat every error the adult made, with the additional error of believing he has avoided them. Adult-Cable's interventions, where available across timelines, take the form of leaving messages, contingencies, and unfinished work that compel the adolescent toward maturity.

**Leverage:** The adolescent is more emotionally volatile, more readable, more baitable than the adult was. The Stryfe lever, the Hope lever, the Scott lever — all function at higher gain on the younger version.

---

## 5.8 — THE DEAD: A LEDGER

A subject of Cable's longevity and operational tempo accumulates dead. The file maintains them not for sentiment but because each functions as ongoing relational variable — Cable converses with his dead operationally, plans around their absences, and metabolizes new losses through the templates the old ones established.

**Madelyne Pryor (biological mother, primary).** Grief unresolved because the object keeps returning in hostile form. He cannot mourn her because the universe will not let her stay mourned.

**Jean Grey (mother-of-rearing, intermittent).** Mourned multiple times. He has stopped fully committing to the grief because the resurrection rate is too high to justify the affective expenditure. This is not callousness; it is triage.

**Stryfe (clone-brother).** Dead in body, present in psyche. The mourning that is owed has never been performed because Cable cannot grieve a man he wanted to kill, and cannot fully celebrate the death of a man who shared his face. The grief is suspended.

**The X-Force dead (Cable's recruits who did not survive his command).** The file does not enumerate them here — see Section 6 — but they reside in this family register because Cable's relationship to them was paternal in structure, and their deaths are filed psychologically alongside the family deaths, not the comrade deaths. He carries them as failed children.

**His own previous selves.** Cable has died and returned. Each return is the death of a prior self. He has, by now, a small population of ghost-selves in his interior, including the adult killed by Kid Cable. He maintains them. He learns from them. He does not, on the record, mourn them — to mourn oneself would be a vanity he does not allow.

**Apocalypse (functional grandfather, in a register that disgusts him).** Apocalypse engineered the conditions of Cable's birth, infected him, raised his clone-brother as heir. To call this a familial relationship is grotesque, but the file registers it as such because Cable's identity is constituted in opposition to Apocalypse the way a son's is constituted in opposition to a father. Apocalypse's various deaths and returns are, for Cable, structurally paternal events — the patriarch will not stay buried.

---

## CROSS-CUTTING OBSERVATIONS

**The family is recursive, not linear.** Cable's parents raised him under disguise. His clone is his brother. His sister is his cult-founder. His alternate self is his rival. His daughter is the only clean line in the genealogy, and he had to go to the future and stay there to keep it clean. The subject's relational architecture is non-Euclidean and he navigates it with the patience of a man who knows there will not be a final family portrait.

**The reparative function is concentrated downstream.** Cable cannot repair his bonds with the generation above him (Scott, Jean, Madelyne) because the temporal mechanics foreclose it. He cannot repair the lateral bonds (Stryfe, Nate Grey, Rachel) because the doubling mechanics distort them. The only register in which repair is available to him is the downstream-paternal register — Hope, X-Force recruits, the younger versions of himself. He has therefore organized his entire emotional life around being a father, because it is the only family position that the universe will let him actually inhabit.

**The leverage map is small and high-yield.** Hope first, Scott second, Stryfe-frame third, Rachel-Askani-frame fourth. Everything else is noise. An actor who controls those four channels controls Cable. An actor who controls none of them will find him impossible to move.

**The pattern that subsumes all the above:** Nathan Summers was given away as an infant by a father who loved him and could not save him. Every relationship in his adult life is a variation on the question *will I be given away again, and by whom, and for what reason that I am supposed to accept*. He has not, on the record, ever been the one to leave first. He dies before he leaves. This is the family pattern. It is also the diagnosis.


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

*Filed under Dossier §7B. Cross-reference §7A (Family of Origin) and §7C (Antagonists & Mirror-Selves). The figures catalogued below constitute the lateral and downward bonds of the subject's life — the people he chose, the people who chose him, and the small handful permitted past the perimeter. The list is short by design. Counter-dependent operators do not accumulate confidants; they curate them.*

---

## 1. DOMINO (Neena Thurman)

**Status:** Sole sustained adult peer attachment of the subject's life. The only person from whom Cable accepts unsolicited correction without renegotiating the relationship.

**Origin and Continuity:** The Six Pack / Wild Pack period (pre-*New Mutants* #87, retconned via *X-Force* #-1, *Cable* #1–4, *Cable & Deadpool* later texture). Domino was a contract operator on Cable's mercenary team in the years he was building cover, funds, and intelligence assets in the present. The original Domino he knew on Six Pack was, for a long stretch, the impostor Copycat (Vanessa Carlysle) — meaning his foundational read of "Neena" was partially a forgery, and the discovery of that forgery is one of the few documented occasions on which the subject visibly reacted with hurt rather than tactical recalibration. The real Domino, recovered from the Mutant Liberation Front, is the woman he has known continuously since.

**Dynamics — granular:**
- *Conversational register.* With every other living person Cable speaks in declarative, instructive, or briefing cadence. With Domino he uses sentence fragments, in-jokes from twenty subjective years of fieldwork, and — uniquely — interrogatives that are not strategic ("you good?"). Surveillance of joint missions records him *asking* her things; this verb is otherwise nearly absent from his speech.
- *Physical proximity tells.* He stands within her probability field in firefights — not for tactical reasons (her luck-field is non-directional) but because it is the only ambient comfort he permits himself. He will sleep in the same room as Domino on op. He will not do this with anyone else, Hope excluded.
- *Touch.* Permitted, infrequent, lateral — shoulder, forearm, back of neck. Never initiated by him in a non-combat context unless she is injured.
- *Humor.* She is the one person who can land a joke on him and watch the corner of his mouth move. He does not laugh; he *concedes* the joke with a quarter-second exhalation. The tell is reliable enough to use as a calibration point.

**Trust / Safety Profile:** Tier 1. She is on the very short list (n ≤ 3) of operators read into his viral status in detail, his Askani heritage in detail, and his time-travel methodology in detail. She has independent access codes to at least two of his safehouse caches. He has named her as Hope's secondary guardian in at least one documented contingency.

**Evolution:** Mercenary partner → tactical peer → only-friend → unconsummated romantic ambient → friend again. The romantic register is real and mutually acknowledged and has been declined by him, repeatedly, because intimacy on his terms would require disclosure of need, and need is the one resource he refuses to spend. Domino, to her credit, has stopped asking and stayed anyway. The relationship is the cleanest evidence in the record that he is *capable* of peer attachment; the unconsummation is the cleanest evidence that the capability is structurally throttled.

**Failure mode:** If Domino were captured, leveraged, or killed, the subject's decision-making would degrade in a specific, predictable direction — he would assume the personal cost himself rather than coordinate a rescue, and he would not tell the team why. The Wild Pack-era Copycat substitution is a rehearsal of this: when he discovered the deception, he ran the recovery op essentially solo.

**Leverage available through this bond:** High, but only by threat to her, never by approach *from* her. She cannot be turned; she can be targeted.

---

## 2. HOPE SUMMERS

*See also Dossier §7D — "Children, Dependents, and Wards" — for the full developmental file. Summary entry only here, because the Hope relationship is the load-bearing column of the subject's late-life psychology and cannot be omitted from any relationship map.*

**Status:** Chosen daughter. The single relationship in which the subject performs the role of parent without role-inversion, asymmetric guilt, or temporal vertigo. The relationship in which he was, by his own metric, successful.

**Duration:** ~16 subjective years on the run across futures (*Messiah CompleX* → *Cable* (2008) #1–25 → *Second Coming*). Continuous, sole-custody, in conditions of escalating viral collapse and pursuit.

**Granular pattern data:**
- *Pedagogy.* Taught her to shoot before she could read. Taught her to read second. This sequence is not casual; it mirrors his own Askani upbringing exactly, and it is the only element of his own childhood he reproduced *deliberately* and without apparent self-criticism — because in his frame the order was correct.
- *Affection delivery.* Forehead contact (his forehead to hers, eyes closed) is the documented gesture; observed on multiple panel-witness occasions. He does not say the word "love" to her on-page until very late in the arc, and even then once. Affection is delivered through provision, vigilance, and the forehead.
- *Self-suppression.* He concealed the rate of his viral decay from her for years. This is the same withholding pattern he runs with all dependents, but with her it carried the specific intent of not making her his nurse.
- *Repair.* The arc *worked.* Hope reaches adulthood attached, capable, ethically grounded, and able to mourn him — i.e., able to do for him the one thing he could never do for his own surrogate parents.

**Failure mode:** None observed in the parenting arc itself. The post-resurrection relationships with grown Hope are uneven — he does not know how to recede from the parental role into peer-adult, and she does not know how to let him.

**Leverage:** Total. She is the one variable that can override any tactical calculation he runs. He knows this and has built operational protocols around it (caches, dead-drops, instructions for who to call when she is in danger and he is not present).

---

## 3. THE SIX PACK / WILD PACK

The mercenary unit Cable ran in the late 1980s of his present-tense operational window. Personnel: **Hammer (Eisenhower Canty), Grizzly (Theodore Winchester), Kane (Garrison Kane), Domino, Cable, and — in rotation — Bridge (G.W. Bridge).** *Cable: Blood and Metal* and *X-Force* #-1 are the canon anchors.

This is the subject's first and last attempt to operate inside a flat-hierarchy adult unit. It ended badly, and the badness is diagnostic.

### 3a. Hammer (Eisenhower Canty)
Crippled in the Iran-contra-coded mission Cable led on the trail of Stryfe. Hammer's spinal injury — left in the field, recovered later — is the wound the subject metabolizes as primary evidence that *he cannot lead peers*. Hammer subsequently turned, joined hostile networks, sold information on Cable to the highest bidder, and the subject *did not retaliate proportionally* — a tell. Cable will kill enemies; he will not kill former teammates he believes he failed. The restraint is not mercy; it is debt.

### 3b. Grizzly (Theodore Winchester)
The Six Pack member with whom Cable maintained the longest residual affection. Big-bodied, simple-presenting, loyal. Killed in the late-90s solo run. The subject was visibly affected — one of the few documented mourning sequences in the file. The pattern: Cable can love the men he commands; he cannot prevent them from dying *because* he commands them.

### 3c. Kane (Garrison Kane)
Cyborg himself, post-mission. Kane's cyberization happened on a Cable op — another teammate's body permanently altered under Cable's command. Kane is the man-shaped record of the subject's professional cost. Their later interactions are cordial, distant, and characterized by Kane's apparent forgiveness and Cable's apparent inability to accept it.

### 3d. Bridge (G.W. Bridge)
The Six Pack member who, after the unit dissolved, became Cable's principal antagonist-from-within-the-establishment — pursuing him as a SHIELD operative for years. The relationship is the cleanest case study of the subject's pattern with **former allies who reattach to institutional authority**: he treats Bridge with professional courtesy, never with hostility, and consistently outmaneuvers him without harming him. When Bridge is killed (much later, by Deadpool, under Hydra-coded circumstances), the subject's reaction is grief, not relief. Bridge is the brother-officer he could not keep but could not unmake.

**Aggregate failure mode of the Six Pack:** The unit ended in a betrayal mission in which Cable, having identified a Stryfe lead, did not disclose his real objective to the team. The team was wounded or killed acting on incomplete intelligence. This is the **founding wound of his command philosophy** and the reason X-Force was, from day one, built around child operatives — not because children are more capable, but because they are *easier to lie to* and the subject had already proven he would lie to adults. He has never said this. The pattern says it.

---

## 4. X-FORCE (The Founding Roster)

Cable's choice of a paramilitary unit composed of late-adolescents — Cannonball, Boom-Boom, Warpath, Shatterstar, Feral, Rictor, briefly Domino as XO — is the most ethically loaded act of his life and the one he has revisited most often in self-criticism. Treat as a single chosen-family unit with sub-entries.

### 4a. Cannonball (Samuel Guthrie)
**Function:** Designated successor. The eldest, the most stable, the agrarian-rooted Kentucky farm boy whose moral compass survives any pressure Cable applies. Cable's elevation of Sam to field command of X-Force, the disclosure (and later qualification) that Sam is an "External" — i.e., immortal — and the explicit grooming for leadership all mark him as the subject's first attempt at *making a successor he himself would not need to be present for*.

**Dynamic:** Mentor-prophet to chosen son. Sam is permitted to argue with him. Sam is, in fact, the only X-Force member who consistently does, and the only one whose arguments occasionally win. Cable does not soften under most criticism; he softens under Sam's because Sam's criticism is delivered without contempt and without fear, the combination of which the subject has encountered perhaps four times in his life.

**Trust:** Tier 2 — read into the broad strokes of Cable's origin but not the operational details of his viral management.

**Failure mode:** If Sam fell, Cable would treat it as confirmation that the project of mentorship is doomed and would harden against future apprentices. This has not been tested. It is the variable to watch.

### 4b. Boom-Boom / Tabitha Smith
**Function:** The X-Force member Cable failed most legibly. A teenage girl with a chaotic-attachment history, recruited into a paramilitary unit by a man who knew better and did it anyway. Cable's relationship with Tabitha is characterized by visible exasperation that is, on close read, displaced self-reproach — she is the embodiment of the X-Force decision being a mistake, and his irritation with her flippancy is irritation with his own decision to put her in fatigues. To his credit, he never broke her; she emerges from his command intact, irreverent, and capable. The credit, in his internal ledger, accrues to her, not him.

### 4c. Warpath (James Proudstar)
**Function:** The X-Force member with the closest analog wound to Cable's own — older brother lost to violence, recruited initially as an assassin, looking for a frame in which to put rage. Cable gave him one, and was honest about it being a frame, not a cure. Their bond is grief-coded and verbally minimal. James is one of the very few people Cable will simply *sit near* without speaking, for long periods.

### 4d. Shatterstar
**Function:** The X-Force member who arrived already a child-soldier from a war-future, i.e., the one whose origin most exactly matched Cable's. The subject's treatment of Shatterstar is interesting precisely because it is *not* preferential — he refuses the mirror, deliberately, because to take Shatterstar as a protégé would be to confirm that he is recruiting his own past selves. Distance, here, is ethical hygiene.

### 4e. Feral, Rictor, and the lesser roster
Catalogued for completeness. Feral's eventual turn to villainy is filed by the subject as another datum in the X-Force-was-a-mistake column. Rictor's trauma profile and seismic instability were never integrated into a long-term arc under Cable's command; the handoff to Peter David's *X-Factor* in later years was, in effect, Cable acknowledging that the boy needed a different father.

**Aggregate evaluation of X-Force:** The subject does not regret X-Force the *unit* — they were, by survival metrics, successful. He regrets X-Force the *decision* — that it was his to make and he made it the way Apocalypse's lieutenants would have. This regret does not appear in dialogue. It appears in his choice, decades later, to raise Hope *alone* rather than build her a unit.

---

## 5. BLAQUESMITH

**Status:** Closest figure to a mentor the adult subject has retained. Askani-affiliated, possibly hyper-evolved, alien-coded, ancient. Operates as Cable's tactical-philosophical interlocutor through much of the solo run (*Cable* vol. 1, 1993–2002).

**Dynamics:** Blaquesmith is the only being who speaks to Cable as an elder. The register is austere — neither warm nor cruel, instructive without affection. Cable accepts the asymmetry because Blaquesmith is one of perhaps three sources of perspective on the Askani heritage that are *not* projection. The relationship is the closest model in the file of *being parented as an adult*, and the subject's compliance with it is information: he is not constitutionally allergic to being mentored, only to being managed.

**Trust:** High on Askani matters; bounded on personal matters. Blaquesmith does not ask after Cable's interior life, which is precisely why the bond functions.

**Failure mode:** Blaquesmith's eventual betrayals/manipulations across the run (the Harbinger arc) cost Cable, and the subject's response was characteristic: he disengaged, did not retaliate proportionally, and integrated the betrayal as confirmation of the rule that mentors leave.

---

## 6. IRENE MERRYWEATHER

**Status:** Civilian journalist, late-90s solo run. The Lois-Lane-shaped slot in Cable's narrative, and instructive precisely because she did not become Lois Lane.

**Function:** Cable allows Irene unprecedented access to his interior — she is the chronicler of the Providence project, the one to whom he explicates the messianic frame in plain language, the only civilian in the file granted that level of confidence. The relationship is **non-sexual, non-romantic, and high-disclosure**, which is a configuration the subject finds easier to sustain than peer-intimacy. Irene functions as something between confessor and biographer. She is the venue in which he speaks aloud the thoughts he otherwise only acts upon.

**Trust:** Tier 2 — content disclosure high, operational disclosure bounded.

**Failure mode:** Irene is endangered repeatedly by proximity. Cable's solution — pull her closer rather than send her away — is anomalous in his pattern and reads as a small experiment in *letting a civilian witness him*. The experiment ended with the Providence collapse and the relationship faded rather than failed.

---

## 7. DEADPOOL (Wade Wilson)

**Status:** The most unlikely sustained relationship in the file. *Cable & Deadpool* (2004–2008) is the canonical text.

**Dynamics:** A man whose entire psychology is hypercontrol, paired with the most uncontrollable possible counterparty. The pairing works because Wade cannot be killed, cannot be lied to effectively (he simply does not care about being lied to), and cannot be managed — meaning he is one of the only people in Cable's life who **cannot be damaged by Cable's withholding.** The asymmetry that wrecks most of the subject's relationships is, with Wade, structurally void.

- *Register.* Cable speaks to Wade in his shortest, driest cadence. Single syllables. Tactical declaratives. Wade fills the silence; Cable does not need to.
- *Touch.* Wade initiates frequently and inappropriately; Cable tolerates it. The tolerance is itself a data point — physical proximity from Wade does not trigger the withdrawal reflex Cable runs with virtually every other adult.
- *Affection.* Cable will not articulate it. He will, however, *show up* for Wade in contexts where the tactical case for doing so is thin, which is his lexicon's equivalent of the word.

**Trust:** Operationally Tier 3 (Wade is a security liability and Cable does not pretend otherwise), but **emotionally** anomalously high, because Wade has nothing to gain by betraying him and has demonstrated the willingness to die alongside him without negotiation.

**Failure mode:** Wade's instability has cost Cable mission objectives repeatedly. The subject's response is the unique non-pattern: he just absorbs the cost and re-plans. He does not, with Wade, run the recrimination protocol he runs with himself. This is one of the more important findings in the relational file — it indicates the subject is *capable* of unconditional regard and has chosen to extend it exactly once, to the person least likely to weaponize it.

---

## 8. STORM (Ororo Munroe), STORM-ADJACENT X-MEN LEADERSHIP

**Function:** Institutional peers from the X-Men proper. The relationship is mutual professional respect bounded by tactical disagreement.

**Pattern:** Cable accepts Storm's authority in joint operations without resistance — interesting, because he accepts almost no one's. The acceptance is grounded in his read of Storm as someone who, like him, was raised inside a religious framework she has had to metabolize critically (her godhood-in-Kenya period; his Askani childhood). They are the only two leaders in the extended X-network who have run sovereign mutant polities, and the bond is partially syndicalist — they have both been heads of state and the experience is non-transferable to anyone who hasn't.

**Trust:** Tier 2, operational. Personal disclosure low.

---

## 9. PROFESSOR XAVIER

**Function:** The mentor-figure he never had and refused to construct retroactively. Filed here rather than under family-of-origin because the relationship is, by Cable's choice, *peer-institutional* rather than filial.

**Dynamics:** Cable has spent his adult life as the **negation** of Xavier's pedagogy — lethal force, child paramilitaries, sovereign nation-building rather than integrationist coexistence — while simultaneously honoring Xavier as the founder. The doubleness is not hypocrisy; it is the considered position of a man who believes Xavier's project was right in spirit and wrong in tooling. After Stryfe shoots Xavier with the T-O virus in *X-Cutioner's Song*, Cable's psionic intervention to save him is the only documented occasion on which the subject has spent his suppression-budget on someone else's life. The cost was substantial. He did not advertise it.

**Trust:** Tier 2 institutional, Tier 4 personal — Cable does not confide in Xavier and does not seek his counsel. The respect is for the office, not the man's access to him.

---

## 10. RACHEL SUMMERS (Askani'thari, half-sister of a sort)

**Function:** The only other living person whose Askani training is real and continuous. *Adventures of Cyclops and Phoenix*, *Askani'son*, and assorted Excalibur/X-Men cross-references.

**Dynamics:** Rachel is — across nested timelines — the founder of the Clan Askani, his nominal great-aunt or older sister depending on which iteration of the Summers genealogy you map, and the woman who as a child befriended his infant self and later, as the Mother Askani, sent him forward to be raised. She is, structurally, his *origin operator*. The relationship is correspondingly weighted: gratitude, residual childhood imprint, theological co-membership, and the particular care of two people who share a vocabulary no one else on Earth uses.

**Trust:** Tier 1 on Askani matters; Tier 2 on the rest. They do not socialize. They do not need to.

**Failure mode:** If Rachel were lost, the Askani lineage in the present day would consist of him alone, and the burden would consolidate. He knows this. It contributes to the long-horizon protectiveness he extends to her without overt expression.

---

## 11. STRYFE — NEGATIVE ENTRY, CROSS-REFERENCED

*See §7C in full. Noted here only because any map of the subject's "chosen family" that omits the brother-clone he could not stop trying to integrate would be incomplete. The annihilatory mirror is, structurally, a relationship, and a not-insignificant fraction of the subject's emotional bandwidth has been spent on it across decades. The pattern: he extends to Stryfe — repeatedly, against evidence — the chance to be brother rather than enemy. Stryfe declines every time. The subject does not stop offering. This is the closest his attachment system gets to behaving pathologically rather than adaptively, and it is filed accordingly.*

---

## 12. THE CONSTELLATION — AGGREGATE PATTERN

Read across the twelve entries above, the relational architecture resolves into a clear shape:

- **One adult peer** (Domino), held at the edge of intimacy that he could close and has chosen not to.
- **One reparative child** (Hope), in whom the developmental contract he was denied was honored in full.
- **One unkillable irritant** (Wade), to whom he extends the unconditional regard he extends nowhere else, *because* it cannot wound him.
- **One ancient mentor** (Blaquesmith), accepted as elder on the grounds of Askani fluency.
- **One founding mentor refused** (Xavier), respected and held at institutional distance.
- **One co-religionist** (Rachel), bonded by shared vocabulary.
- **One unit of grown men he failed** (Six Pack), debt unrepaid by design.
- **One unit of children he commanded and largely got home** (X-Force), the moral ledger of which he is still working.
- **One civilian witness** (Irene), the experiment in being seen.
- **One peer head-of-state** (Storm), the only person who has held the same office.
- **One annihilatory mirror** (Stryfe), to whom he keeps extending the brotherhood that is the central wound of his life.

The list is small. The list is sufficient. The subject runs a sparse network of high-tensile bonds and would not survive — operationally or psychologically — a denser one. Surveillance posture should account for the fact that **the list of people who could move him is shorter than the list of people who could kill him**, and is therefore the higher-value target inventory.

*— End §7B.


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

*An exhaustive mapping of the antagonistic and intimate fields around the subject. These two categories are presented together because, in this subject's life, the boundary between them is unstable: his enemies have lived inside him at the cellular level, and his lovers have, without exception, been combat partners first. He has never had a civilian intimate. He has never had an enemy who did not, in some way, share his genome, his project, or his bed.*

---

## I. RIVALS & ENEMIES

### 1. Stryfe — the Mirror Self

**Origin and structural relation.** Stryfe is the genetically identical clone made by Apocalypse's forces when the infant Nathan was abducted from the Clan Askani. The clone was raised as the heir-apparent in Apocalypse's court; Nathan was raised by the resistance. Two bodies, one genome, opposite educations. They are not brothers in any ordinary sense. They are the same person under two upbringings, run as a controlled experiment by their shared father-figure.

**History of contact.** First encounter in the present: Stryfe leads the Mutant Liberation Front, Cable opposes him with the New Mutants/X-Force. Years of cat-and-mouse before the *X-Cutioner's Song* arc, in which Stryfe shoots Xavier with a T-O virus, frames Cable for it, and during the climax reveals to Cable that Cable was the clone, not him — a lie engineered for maximum identity damage. Cable spends a substantial portion of the subsequent solo run unable to determine which of them was the original. Even after the question is formally resolved, the doubt persists. Stryfe's consciousness, post-physical-death, intermittently colonizes Cable's mind across multiple arcs — the enemy is not external.

**Dynamics.** Stryfe is the externalized container for every part of the subject he has refused to become: the warlord, the entitled messiah, the avenger who would let the world burn for personal grievance, the child who never got past being a weapon. The feud is therefore unresolvable by combat, because combat is precisely what Stryfe *is*. Every time the subject defeats him in the field, he loses internally — because killing Stryfe means killing the disowned content, which always returns.

**Trust / safety.** Zero. Negative, in the technical sense — Stryfe is the one entity in the world who knows the subject's somatic vulnerabilities (they share them) and who has, at points, literally been inside his nervous system. The subject must operate at all times with a residual psychic firewall against him, which costs energy that could otherwise have gone to virus containment. Stryfe is, in measurable telekinetic terms, a tax on the subject's life expectancy.

**Failure mode.** When the subject engages Stryfe he becomes uncharacteristically reactive — quicker to lethal force, slower to strategy. The only enemy who can pull him out of his long-game discipline. He has, multiple times, broken his own operational rules to pursue Stryfe.

**Leverage.** Stryfe holds the original injury — *which one of us is real* — and can reopen it at will. The subject holds nothing reciprocal except superior moral clarity, which Stryfe does not register as a loss.

---

### 2. Apocalypse / En Sabah Nur — the Architect

**Origin.** The reason for the subject's entire existence. Cable was conceived as a weapon against Apocalypse; Apocalypse infected him with the T-O virus as an infant. In a sense, Apocalypse made him twice — once by being the threat that necessitated his designed conception, and once by infecting the body that resulted. The subject's body, his mission, his pain, and his future tense all bear Apocalypse's fingerprint.

**Dynamics.** Unlike Stryfe (intimate, mirroring, intrapsychic), Apocalypse is structural. He is the principle the subject was made to negate: the doctrine that selection produces value, that the weak are owed nothing, that children may be designed as instruments. The horror for the subject is that *he himself* is a product of selection logic — Mr. Sinister bred him for purpose. The opposition to Apocalypse is therefore opposition to the conditions of his own birth. To win is to declare himself an exception to the rule that produced him.

**History of contact.** Numerous, time-displaced, often non-linear. Cable has fought Apocalypse in the present, in the far future, and across several alternate timelines. There is no first or final battle, because the relationship is causally circular: each is, in some timeline, the other's predicate.

**Trust / safety.** Asymmetric: the subject has occasionally been forced into temporary alignment (when there is a greater threat) and tolerates it; Apocalypse views the subject as a defective prototype that should have been culled in infancy. The subject knows this and uses it — Apocalypse consistently underestimates him because the underestimation is doctrinal.

**Failure mode.** The subject can be drawn into the *terms* of Apocalypse's framework — the "only the strong survive, therefore I must be stronger" logic — without noticing he has accepted them. The X-Force years are partly this failure: a Darwinian counter-Apocalypse, fighting the doctrine by adopting its methods. The subject has admitted this internally; it is one of the few places he allows himself ongoing guilt.

**Leverage.** Apocalypse can, at any time, reframe the subject's existence as Apocalypse's product. The subject answers this by sustained refusal to act as a product. The leverage is therefore neutralized only by behavior, never by argument, and must be neutralized continuously.

---

### 3. Mister Sinister / Nathaniel Essex — the Designer

**Origin.** The man who engineered Madelyne Pryor specifically to gestate this subject. Sinister did not create Cable, but he created the conditions for Cable's creation, by manipulating Scott Summers' lineage across generations to produce a specific genetic outcome. The subject is, literally, Sinister's experiment.

**Dynamics.** Where Apocalypse is the threat the subject was built *against*, Sinister is the hand that did the building. The subject's relationship to him is cooler, more clinical, and arguably more damaged in a quiet way — Apocalypse is a hated enemy, Sinister is the unwanted parent-of-record. The subject does not want to be in a room with him.

**Trust / safety.** Zero, but transactional in a way the Stryfe and Apocalypse relationships are not. Sinister is, periodically, a useful information source — he knows the subject's biology better than the subject does — and the subject has, in extremis, taken information from him. Each such transaction costs him something he does not name.

**Failure mode.** The subject tends to *not engage* Sinister when other strategies are available. This is uncharacteristic; it is a tell. He avoids the man who knows the recipe.

**Leverage.** Sinister holds the genetic ledger. He can, in theory, produce more Nathans, more Madelynes, more variations on the experiment. The subject's response to this leverage is to ensure that any future Sinister-derived being is treated as a person rather than as an iteration — which is, again, behavior rather than argument.

---

### 4. Bishop — the Hunter

**Origin.** A time-displaced mutant from a different future who, during the *Messiah Complex* and *Cable* (2008) runs, becomes convinced that the infant Hope Summers will grow up to cause the catastrophe that destroyed his timeline. He pursues Cable and Hope across centuries with the explicit intention of murdering the child.

**Dynamics.** This is the cleanest mirror-rivalry in the subject's life after Stryfe, and arguably the more painful one because Bishop is not evil. Bishop is a soldier with a future to prevent and a child to kill to prevent it — which is, structurally, what *Cable* was, in his original mission framing. Cable was sent back to prevent Apocalypse; Bishop is doing the equivalent. The difference is that Cable refused to make the child the target, and Bishop did not. The chase through time is one ethical man pursuing another along divergent applications of the same logic.

**History of contact.** Years of pursuit across temporally degraded futures — burned-out cities, irradiated wastes, decaying tech. The subject is fleeing with an infant who becomes a child, and he ages substantially during the chase. Bishop pursues with a singularity of purpose that the subject recognizes from his own younger self.

**Trust / safety.** Combat-only after Hope. Prior to the Messiah arcs there had been provisional collegiality; after, none. The subject would kill Bishop without hesitation in defense of Hope and has come close.

**Failure mode.** The chase nearly cost the subject Hope multiple times, and accelerated the T-O viral progression that eventually killed him. Bishop, by forcing the subject to run continuously, weaponized the subject's own body against him.

**Leverage.** Bishop's leverage was Hope herself — he could not be ignored; the subject had to keep moving. The subject's only counter-leverage was endurance, which is to say: time, which is to say: his own life. He spent it.

---

### 5. The Mutant Liberation Front (MLF) — Stryfe's Operational Wing

**Composition and history.** A roster including Reignfire, Wildside, Forearm, Strobe, Tempo, Dragoness, Kamikaze, Zero — Stryfe's lieutenants and field operatives, often the direct opponents of X-Force in its earliest years. After Stryfe's apparent death, factions of the MLF persisted under Reignfire and others.

**Dynamics.** For the subject, the MLF was the training ground on which X-Force was *forged*. They were the enemy that justified, in his own eyes, the recruitment and weaponization of teenagers. There is therefore a quiet, persistent gratitude in his hatred — without an MLF to fight, X-Force would have had no charter, and the subject would have had to confront his need to surround himself with subordinate soldiers without a war to justify it.

**Trust / safety.** None individually. Operationally, the subject understood them well enough to predict their tactics for years.

**Failure mode.** Engagement with the MLF could draw the subject into pre-planned Stryfe theatrics — most of the MLF's deployments were bait.

**Leverage.** Minimal. They were instruments, like he was.

---

### 6. William Stryker, the Reavers, and the Anti-Mutant Apparatus — Generic Enemies

The subject has cycled through standard X-universe antagonists (Reavers, Purifiers, government black ops, the Hellfire Cult during *Messiah War*, etc.) at a frequency that makes individual enumeration low-yield. The relevant pattern is *operational*: the subject treats these enemies as logistics problems rather than personal ones. He does not invest in them. He does not feud. He removes them and moves on. The lack of investment is itself diagnostic — his hatred is a finite resource and is reserved for enemies who implicate his identity.

---

### 7. Deadpool / Wade Wilson — the Antagonist Who Became Something Else

**Origin.** Initial encounters were antagonistic — Deadpool, in his Weapon X / mercenary period, ran missions against the subject's operations. Their physiologies are linked: Deadpool's healing factor was derived in part from research adjacent to the T-O containment problem.

**Dynamics.** Over decades of narrative, the relationship has migrated from rivalry through grudging tactical cooperation to a persistent, surprisingly stable buddy dyad — *Cable & Deadpool* as a sustained run. This is one of only two long-form peer relationships the subject sustains (the other is Domino). The dynamic works because Deadpool occupies a role no one else in the subject's life can: he is the only person who is allowed to *laugh* at him, and the only person who refuses to take the subject's messianism seriously. The subject needs this and cannot ask for it.

**Trust / safety.** High operationally, low emotionally — but in an unusual configuration. The subject trusts Deadpool with his life and not with his secrets, which is reversed from the standard pattern. The friendship runs on combat reliability and on a shared understanding of what it is to inhabit a body that is also a battlefield.

**Failure mode.** Deadpool can — and does — sabotage the subject's plans for reasons of conscience, comedy, or both. The subject tolerates this at a level he tolerates from nobody else, including Domino.

**Leverage.** Reciprocal and roughly equal. They have killed each other and gotten over it.
